<p>The <b>Tour de France</b> (<small>French pronunciation:&#160;​</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[tuʁ də fʁɑ̃s]</a></span>) is an annual <a href="/wiki/Stage_race" class="mw-redirect" title="Stage race">multiple stage</a> <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_racing" class="mw-redirect" title="Bicycle racing">bicycle race</a> primarily held in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Telegraph_Tour_Info_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph_Tour_Info-1">[1]</a></sup> while also occasionally making passes through nearby countries. Like the other <a href="/wiki/Grand_Tour_(cycling)" title="Grand Tour (cycling)">Grand Tours</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Giro_d%27Italia" title="Giro d'Italia">Giro d'Italia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vuelta_a_Espa%C3%B1a" title="Vuelta a España">Vuelta a España</a>), it consists of 21 stages over a little more than 3 weeks.</p>
<p>The race was first organized in <a href="/wiki/1903_Tour_de_France" title="1903 Tour de France">1903</a> to increase sales for the newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/L%27Auto" class="mw-redirect" title="L'Auto">L'Auto</a></i>;<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> which is currently run by the <a href="/wiki/Amaury_Sport_Organisation" title="Amaury Sport Organisation">Amaury Sport Organisation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The race has been held annually since its first edition in 1903 except when it was stopped for the two <a href="/wiki/World_war" title="World war">World Wars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-reveal_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reveal-4">[4]</a></sup> As the Tour gained prominence and popularity, the race was lengthened and its reach began to extend around the globe. Participation expanded from a primarily French field, as riders from all over the world began to participate in the race each year. The Tour is a <a href="/wiki/UCI_World_Tour" title="UCI World Tour">UCI World Tour</a> event, which means that the teams that compete in the race are mostly <a href="/wiki/UCI_WorldTeam" title="UCI WorldTeam">UCI WorldTeams</a>, with the exception of the teams that the organizers invite.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Traditionally, the race is held primarily in the month of July. While the route changes each year, the format of the race stays the same with the appearance of time trials,<sup id="cite_ref-Telegraph_Tour_Info_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph_Tour_Info-1">[1]</a></sup> the passage through the mountain chains of the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>, and the finish on the <a href="/wiki/Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es_stage_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Champs-Élysées stage in the Tour de France">Champs-Élysées</a> in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The modern editions of the Tour de France consist of 21 day-long segments (stages) over a 23-day period and cover around 3,500 kilometres (2,200&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The race alternates between clockwise and counterclockwise circuits of France.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199617_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199617-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are usually between 20 and 22 teams, with nine riders in each. All of the stages are timed to the finish; the riders' times are compounded with their previous stage times.<sup id="cite_ref-Telegraph_Tour_Info_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph_Tour_Info-1">[1]</a></sup> The rider with the lowest cumulative finishing times is the leader of the race and wears the <i><a href="/wiki/Maillot_jaune" class="mw-redirect" title="Maillot jaune">maillot jaune</a></i> or yellow jersey.<sup id="cite_ref-Telegraph_Tour_Info_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph_Tour_Info-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ASO_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ASO-11">[11]</a></sup> While the general classification garners the most attention, there are other contests held within the Tour: the <a href="/wiki/Points_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Points classification in the Tour de France">points classification</a> for the sprinters, the <a href="/wiki/Mountains_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Mountains classification in the Tour de France">mountains classification</a> for the climbers, <a href="/wiki/Young_rider_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Young rider classification in the Tour de France">young rider classification</a> for riders under the age of 26, and the <a href="/wiki/Team_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Team classification in the Tour de France">team classification</a> for the fastest teams.<sup id="cite_ref-Telegraph_Tour_Info_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph_Tour_Info-1">[1]</a></sup> Achieving a stage win also provides prestige, often accomplished by a team's <a href="/wiki/Cycling_sprinter" class="mw-redirect" title="Cycling sprinter">cycling sprinter</a> specialist.</p>
<p></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">Origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Tour de France was created in 1903. The roots of the Tour de France trace back to the emergence of two rival sports newspapers in the country. On one hand was <i><a href="/wiki/Le_V%C3%A9lo" title="Le Vélo">Le Vélo</a></i>, the first and the largest daily sports newspaper in France<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoeufL.C3.A9onard200323_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoeufL.C3.A9onard200323-12">[12]</a></sup> which sold 80,000 copies a day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson1991_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson1991-13">[13]</a></sup> On the other was <i><a href="/wiki/L%27Auto" class="mw-redirect" title="L'Auto">L'Auto</a></i>, which had been set-up by journalists and business-people including Comte <a href="/wiki/Jules-Albert_de_Dion" title="Jules-Albert de Dion">Jules-Albert de Dion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adolphe_Cl%C3%A9ment" class="mw-redirect" title="Adolphe Clément">Adolphe Clément</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard_Michelin_(born_1859)" class="mw-redirect" title="Édouard Michelin (born 1859)">Édouard Michelin</a> in 1899. The rival paper emerged following disagreements over the <a href="/wiki/Dreyfus_Affair" class="mw-redirect" title="Dreyfus Affair">Dreyfus Affair</a>, a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cause_c%C3%A9l%C3%A8bre" class="extiw" title="wikt:cause célèbre">cause célèbre</a> (in which de Dion was implicated) that divided France at the end of the 19th century over the innocence of <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Dreyfus" title="Alfred Dreyfus">Alfred Dreyfus</a>, a French army officer convicted—though later exonerated—of selling military secrets to the Germans.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[n 1]</a></sup> The new newspaper appointed <a href="/wiki/Henri_Desgrange" title="Henri Desgrange">Henri Desgrange</a> as the editor. He was a prominent cyclist and owner with Victor Goddet of the <a href="/wiki/Velodrome" title="Velodrome">velodrome</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Parc_des_Princes" title="Parc des Princes">Parc des Princes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoddet199116_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoddet199116-15">[14]</a></sup> De Dion knew him through his cycling reputation, through the books and cycling articles that he had written, and through press articles he had written for the Clément tyre company.</p>
<p><i>L'Auto</i> was not the success its backers wanted. Stagnating sales lower than the rival it was intended to surpass led to a crisis meeting on 20 November 1902 on the middle floor of <i>L'Auto'</i>s office at 10 Rue du Faubourg Montmartre, Paris. The last to speak was the most junior there, the chief cycling journalist, a 26-year-old named <a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9o_Lef%C3%A8vre" title="Géo Lefèvre">Géo Lefèvre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007-16">[15]</a></sup> Desgrange had poached him from Giffard's paper.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoddet199120_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoddet199120-17">[16]</a></sup> Lefèvre suggested a six-day race of the sort popular on the track but all around France.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoddet199120_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoddet199120-17">[16]</a></sup> Long-distance cycle races were a popular means to sell more newspapers, but nothing of the length that Lefèvre suggested had been attempted.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[n 2]</a></sup> If it succeeded, it would help <i>L'Auto</i> match its rival and perhaps put it out of business.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare201364_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare201364-19">[17]</a></sup> It could, as Desgrange said, "nail Giffard's beak shut."<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup> Desgrange and Lefèvre discussed it after lunch. Desgrange was doubtful but the paper's financial director, Victor Goddet, was enthusiastic. He handed Desgrange the keys to the company safe and said: "Take whatever you need."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoddet199115_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoddet199115-22">[20]</a></sup> <i>L'Auto</i> announced the race on 19 January 1903.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_first_Tour_de_France">The first Tour de France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: The first Tour de France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1903_Tour_de_France" title="1903 Tour de France">1903 Tour de France</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eerste_Tour_de_France_-_First_Tour_de_France.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Eerste_Tour_de_France_-_First_Tour_de_France.jpg/170px-Eerste_Tour_de_France_-_First_Tour_de_France.jpg" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Eerste_Tour_de_France_-_First_Tour_de_France.jpg/255px-Eerste_Tour_de_France_-_First_Tour_de_France.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Eerste_Tour_de_France_-_First_Tour_de_France.jpg/340px-Eerste_Tour_de_France_-_First_Tour_de_France.jpg 2x" data-file-width="544" data-file-height="724" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eerste_Tour_de_France_-_First_Tour_de_France.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Maurice_Garin" title="Maurice Garin">Maurice Garin</a>, winner of the first Tour de France standing on the right. The man on the left is possibly <a href="/wiki/Leon_Georget" class="mw-redirect" title="Leon Georget">Leon Georget</a> (1903)<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first Tour de France was staged in 1903. The plan was a five-stage race from 31 May to 5 July, starting in Paris and stopping in Lyon, Marseille, Bordeaux and Nantes before returning to Paris. Toulouse was added later to break the long haul across <a href="/wiki/Southern_France" title="Southern France">southern France</a> from the Mediterranean to the Atlantic. Stages would go through the night and finish next afternoon, with rest days before riders set off again. But this proved too daunting and the costs too great for most<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare201313_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare201313-24">[22]</a></sup> and only 15 competitors had entered. Desgrange had never been wholly convinced and he came close to dropping the idea.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson199144_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson199144-25">[23]</a></sup> Instead, he cut the length to 19 days, changed the dates to 1 to 19 July, and offered a daily allowance to those who averaged at least 20&#160;km/h on all the stages,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThierryChany201121_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThierryChany201121-26">[24]</a></sup> equivalent to what a rider would have expected to earn each day had he worked in a factory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013131_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013131-27">[25]</a></sup> He also cut the entry fee from 20 to 10 francs and set the first prize at 12,000 francs and the prize for each day's winner at 3,000 francs. The winner would thereby win six times what most workers earned in a year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013131_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013131-27">[25]</a></sup> That attracted between 60 and 80 entrants&#160;– the higher number may have included serious inquiries and some who dropped out&#160;– among them not just professionals but amateurs, some unemployed, some simply adventurous.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007-16">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Desgrange seems not to have forgotten the <a href="/wiki/Dreyfus_Affair" class="mw-redirect" title="Dreyfus Affair">Dreyfus Affair</a> that launched his race and raised the passions of his backers. He announced his new race on 1 July 1903 by citing the writer <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Zola" title="Émile Zola">Émile Zola</a>, whose open letter in which every paragraph started" <i><a href="/wiki/J%27accuse" class="mw-redirect" title="J'accuse">J'accuse</a>&#160;...</i>" led to Dreyfus's acquittal, establishing the florid style he used henceforth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare201363_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare201363-28">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre19967_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre19967-29">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson199145_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson199145-30">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first Tour de France started almost outside the Café Reveil-Matin at the junction of the Melun and Corbeil roads in the village of Montgeron. It was waved away by the starter, Georges Abran, at 3:16 p.m. on 1 July 1903. <i>L'Auto</i> hadn't featured the race on its front page that morning.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[n 3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThierryChany201126_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThierryChany201126-32">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllchinBell20033_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllchinBell20033-33">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among the competitors were the eventual winner, <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Garin" title="Maurice Garin">Maurice Garin</a>, his well-built rival <a href="/wiki/Hippolyte_Aucouturier" title="Hippolyte Aucouturier">Hippolyte Aucouturier</a>, the German favourite <a href="/wiki/Josef_Fischer_(cyclist)" title="Josef Fischer (cyclist)">Josef Fischer</a>, and a collection of adventurers including one competing as "Samson".<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[n 4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many riders dropped out of the race after completing the initial stages as the physical effort the tour required was just too much. Only a mere 24 entrants remained at the end of the fourth stage.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[31]</a></sup> The race finished on the edge of Paris at Ville d'Avray, outside the Restaurant du Père Auto, before a ceremonial ride into Paris and several laps of the Parc des Princes. Garin dominated the race, winning the first and last two stages, at 25.68&#160;km/h. The last rider, Millocheau, finished 64h 47m 22s behind him.</p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/L%27Auto-Journal" title="L'Auto-Journal">L'Auto</a></i><a href="/wiki/L%27Auto-Journal" title="L'Auto-Journal">'</a>s mission was accomplished as throughout the race circulation of the publication doubled, making the race something much larger than Desgrange had ever hoped for.</p>
<h3><span id="1904–1939"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1904.E2.80.931939">1904–1939</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1904–1939">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Such was the passion that the first Tour created in spectators and riders that Desgrange said the <a href="/wiki/1904_Tour_de_France" title="1904 Tour de France">1904 Tour de France</a> would be the last. Cheating was rife and riders were beaten up by rival fans as they neared the top of the col de la République, sometimes called the col du Grand Bois, outside St-Étienne.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGannMcGann200611_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGannMcGann200611-36">[32]</a></sup> The leading riders, including the winner Maurice Garin, were disqualified, though it took the Union Vélocipèdique de France until 30 November to make the decision.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeray2000154_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeray2000154-37">[33]</a></sup> McGann says the UVF waited so long "...well aware of the passions aroused by the race."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGannMcGann200612_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGannMcGann200612-38">[34]</a></sup> Desgrange's opinion of the fighting and cheating showed in the headline of his reaction in <i>L'Auto</i>: THE END.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeray2000129_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeray2000129-39">[35]</a></sup> Desgrange's despair did not last. By the following spring he was planning another Tour, longer at 11 stages rather than 6 – and this time all in daylight to make any cheating more obvious.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeray2000148_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeray2000148-40">[36]</a></sup> Stages in 1905 began between 3&#160;am and 7:30&#160;am.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre19969_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre19969-41">[37]</a></sup> The race captured the imagination. <i>L'Auto's</i> circulation rose from 25,000 to 65,000;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007-16">[15]</a></sup> by 1908 it was a quarter of a million. The Tour returned after its suspension during <a href="/wiki/World_War_One" class="mw-redirect" title="World War One">World War One</a> and continued to grow, with circulation of <i>L'Auto</i> reaching 500,000 by 1923. The record claimed by Desgrange was 854,000 during the 1933 Tour.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[38]</a></sup> <i>Le Vélo</i>, meanwhile, went out of business in 1904.</p>
<p>Desgrange and his Tour invented <a href="/wiki/Race_stage" title="Race stage">bicycle stage racing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGannMcGann2006_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGannMcGann2006-43">[39]</a></sup> Desgrange experimented with different ways of judging the winner. Initially he used total accumulated time (as used in the modern Tour de France) <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre19967_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre19967-29">[27]</a></sup> but from 1906 to 1912 by points for placings each day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre19969_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre19969-41">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[n 5]</a></sup> Desgrange saw problems in judging both by time and by points. By time, a rider coping with a mechanical problem—which the rules insisted he repair alone—could lose so much time that it cost him the race. Equally, riders could finish so separated that time gained or lost on one or two days could decide the whole race. Judging the race by points removed over-influential time differences but discouraged competitors from riding hard. It made no difference whether they finished fast or slow or separated by seconds or hours, so they were inclined to ride together at a relaxed pace until close to the line, only then disputing the final placings that would give them points. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre19969_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre19969-41">[37]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Btv1b8442891c-p21_(2).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Btv1b8442891c-p21_%282%29.jpg/220px-Btv1b8442891c-p21_%282%29.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Btv1b8442891c-p21_%282%29.jpg/330px-Btv1b8442891c-p21_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Btv1b8442891c-p21_%282%29.jpg/440px-Btv1b8442891c-p21_%282%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1632" data-file-height="1146" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Btv1b8442891c-p21_(2).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1936 Tour de France</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The format changed over time. The Tour originally ran around the perimeter of France. Cycling was an endurance sport and the organisers realised the sales they would achieve by creating supermen of the competitors. Night riding was dropped after the second Tour in 1904, when there had been persistent cheating when judges could not see riders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeray2000_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeray2000-45">[40]</a></sup> That reduced the daily and overall distance but the emphasis remained on endurance. Desgrange said his ideal race would be so hard that only one rider would make it to Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[41]</a></sup> The first mountain stages (in the <a href="/wiki/Pyrenees" title="Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a>) appeared in <a href="/wiki/1910_Tour_de_France" title="1910 Tour de France">1910</a>. Early tours had long multi-day stages, with the format settling on 15 stages from <a href="/wiki/1910_Tour_de_France" title="1910 Tour de France">1910</a> until <a href="/wiki/1924_Tour_de_France" title="1924 Tour de France">1924</a>. After this stages were gradually shortened, such that by 1936 there were as many as three stages in a single day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199636_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199636-47">[42]</a></sup> Desgrange initially preferred to see the Tour as a race of individuals. The first Tours were open to whoever wanted to compete. Most riders were in teams that looked after them. The private entrants were called <i>touriste-routiers</i>&#160;– tourists of the road&#160;– from 1923<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199623_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199623-48">[43]</a></sup> and were allowed to take part provided they make no demands on the organisers. Some of the Tour's most colourful characters have been touriste-routiers. One finished each day's race and then performed acrobatic tricks in the street to raise the price of a hotel. Until 1925 Desgrange forbade team members from pacing each other.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199625_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199625-49">[44]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1927_Tour_de_France" title="1927 Tour de France">1927</a> and <a href="/wiki/1928_Tour_de_France" title="1928 Tour de France">1928</a> Tours, however, consisted mainly of <a href="/wiki/Team_time-trial" class="mw-redirect" title="Team time-trial">team time-trials</a>, an unsuccessful experiment which sought to avoid a proliferation of sprint finishes on flat stages. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199627_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199627-50">[45]</a></sup> Desgrange was a traditionalist with equipment. Until <a href="/wiki/1930_Tour_de_France" title="1930 Tour de France">1930</a> he demanded that riders mend their bicycles without help and that they use the same bicycle from start to end. Exchanging a damaged bicycle for another was allowed only in 1923.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199623_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199623-48">[43]</a></sup> Desgrange stood against the use of multiple gears and for many years insisted riders use wooden rims, fearing the heat of braking while coming down mountains would melt the glue that held the tires on metal rims (they were finally allowed in 1937).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199637_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199637-51">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the end of the 1920s, Desgrange believed he could not beat what he believed were the underhand tactics of bike factories.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaso200350_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaso200350-52">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcGannMcGann200684_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcGannMcGann200684-53">[48]</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Alcyon" title="Alcyon">Alcyon</a> team contrived to get <a href="/wiki/Maurice_De_Waele" title="Maurice De Waele">Maurice De Waele</a> to win even though he was sick,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199630_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199630-54">[49]</a></sup> he said "My race has been won by a corpse".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199630_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199630-54">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[50]</a></sup> In 1930 Desgrange again attempted to take control of the Tour from teams, insisting competitors enter in national teams rather than trade teams and that competitors ride plain yellow bicycles that he would provide, without a maker's name.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199630_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199630-54">[49]</a></sup> There was no place for individuals in the post-1930s teams and so Desgrange created regional teams, generally from France, to take in riders who would not otherwise have qualified. The original touriste-routiers mostly disappeared but some were absorbed into regional teams. In 1936 Desgrange had a prostate operation. At the time, two operations were needed; the Tour de France was due to fall between them. Desgrange persuaded his surgeon to let him follow the race.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoddet1991_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoddet1991-56">[51]</a></sup> The second day proved too much and, in a fever at <a href="/wiki/Charleville-M%C3%A9zi%C3%A8res" title="Charleville-Mézières">Charleville</a>, he retired to his château at Beauvallon. Desgrange died at home on the Mediterranean coast on 16 August 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoddet1991_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoddet1991-56">[51]</a></sup> The race was taken over by his deputy, <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Goddet" title="Jacques Goddet">Jacques Goddet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[52]</a></sup> The Tour was again disrupted by War after 1939, and did not return until <a href="/wiki/1947_Tour_de_France" title="1947 Tour de France">1947</a>.</p>
<h3><span id="1947–1969"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1947.E2.80.931969">1947–1969</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1947–1969">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jacques_Goddet_Memorial.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Jacques_Goddet_Memorial.jpg/170px-Jacques_Goddet_Memorial.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Jacques_Goddet_Memorial.jpg/255px-Jacques_Goddet_Memorial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Jacques_Goddet_Memorial.jpg/340px-Jacques_Goddet_Memorial.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1932" data-file-height="2580" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jacques_Goddet_Memorial.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Jacques_Goddet" title="Jacques Goddet">Jacques Goddet</a> memorial at the top of the <a href="/wiki/Col_du_Tourmalet" title="Col du Tourmalet">Col du Tourmalet</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1944, <i>L'Auto</i> was closed&#160;– its doors nailed shut&#160;– and its belongings, including the Tour, sequestrated by the state for publishing articles too close to the Germans.<sup id="cite_ref-Liberation1_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liberation1-58">[53]</a></sup> Rights to the Tour were therefore owned by the government. Jacques Goddet was allowed to publish another daily sports paper, <i>L'Équipe</i>, but there was a rival candidate to run the Tour: a consortium of <i>Sports</i> and <i>Miroir Sprint</i>. Each organised a candidate race. <i>L'Équipe</i> and <i>Le Parisien Libéré</i> had La Course du Tour de France<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[54]</a></sup> and <i>Sports</i> and <i>Miroir Sprint</i> had La Ronde de France. Both were five stages, the longest the government would allow because of shortages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013-60">[55]</a></sup> <i>L'Équipe</i>'s race was better organised and appealed more to the public because it featured national teams that had been successful before the war, when French cycling was at a high. <i>L'Équipe</i> was given the right to organise the <a href="/wiki/1947_Tour_de_France" title="1947 Tour de France">1947 Tour de France</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoddet1991_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoddet1991-56">[51]</a></sup> However, <i>L'Équipe'</i>s finances were never sound and Goddet accepted an advance by Émilion Amaury, who had supported his bid to run the post-war Tour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoddet1991_56-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoddet1991-56">[51]</a></sup> Amaury was a newspaper magnate whose condition was that his sports editor, <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9lix_L%C3%A9vitan" title="Félix Lévitan">Félix Lévitan</a> should join Goddet for the Tour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoddet1991_56-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoddet1991-56">[51]</a></sup> The two worked together, Goddet running the sporting side and Lévitan the financial.</p>
<p>On the Tour's return, the format of the race settled on between 20-25 stages. Most stages would last one day but the scheduling of 'split' stages continued well in to the 1980s. 1953 saw the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Points_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Points classification in the Tour de France">Green Jersey</a> 'Points' competition. National teams contested the Tour until 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199655_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199655-61">[56]</a></sup> The teams were of different sizes. Some nations had more than one team and some were mixed in with others to make up the number. National teams caught the public imagination but had a snag: that riders might normally have been in rival trade teams the rest of the season. The loyalty of riders was sometimes questionable, within and between teams. Sponsors were always unhappy about releasing their riders into anonymity for the biggest race of the year, as riders in national teams wore the colours of their country and a small cloth panel on their chest that named the team for which they normally rode. The situation became critical at the start of the 1960s. Sales of bicycles had fallen and bicycle factories were closing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaso2003122_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaso2003122-62">[57]</a></sup> There was a risk, the trade said, that the industry would die if factories were not allowed the publicity of the Tour de France. The Tour returned to trade teams in 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199655_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199655-61">[56]</a></sup> In the same year, Émilion Amaury, owner of <i>le Parisien Libéré</i>, became financially involved in the Tour. He made <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9lix_L%C3%A9vitan" title="Félix Lévitan">Félix Lévitan</a> co-organizer of the Tour, and it was decided that Levitan would focus on the financial issues, and <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Goddet" title="Jacques Goddet">Jacques Goddet</a> on the sporting issues.<sup id="cite_ref-Gann-1962_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gann-1962-63">[58]</a></sup> The Tour de France was meant for professional cyclists, but in 1961 the organisation started the <a href="/wiki/Tour_de_l%27Avenir" title="Tour de l'Avenir">Tour de l'Avenir</a> the amateur version.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013115_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013115-64">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Doping had become a problem culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Tom_Simpson" title="Death of Tom Simpson">death of Tom Simpson</a> in 1967, after which riders went on strike, <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199759_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199759-65">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENicholson199150_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENicholson199150-66">[61]</a></sup> though the organisers suspected sponsors provoked them. The <a href="/wiki/Union_Cycliste_Internationale" title="Union Cycliste Internationale">Union Cycliste Internationale</a> introduced limits to daily and overall distances, imposed rest days and tests were introduced for riders. It was then impossible to follow the frontiers, and the Tour increasingly zig-zagged across the country, sometimes with unconnected days' races linked by train, while still maintaining some sort of loop. The Tour returned to national teams for 1967 and 1968<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199660_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199660-67">[62]</a></sup> as "an experiment".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMaso2003126_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaso2003126-68">[63]</a></sup> The Tour returned to trade teams in 1969<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199662_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199662-69">[64]</a></sup> with a suggestion that national teams could come back every few years, but this has not happened since.</p>
<h3><span id="1969–1987"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1969.E2.80.931987">1969–1987</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1969–1987">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the early 1970s the race was dominated by <a href="/wiki/Eddy_Merckx" title="Eddy Merckx">Eddy Merckx</a>, who won the General Classification five times, the <a href="/wiki/Mountains_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Mountains classification in the Tour de France">Mountains Classification</a> twice, the <a href="/wiki/Points_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Points classification in the Tour de France">Points Classification</a> three times and a record 34 stages.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>During this era race director Felix Lévitan brought in a new commercial era of the Tour, beginning to recruit sponsors, sometimes accepting prizes in kind if he could not get cash.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199669_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199669-71">[66]</a></sup> He introduced the finish of the Tour at the Avenue des <a href="/wiki/Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es" title="Champs-Élysées">Champs-Élysées</a> in 1975, the same year the polka-dot jersey (in the colors of a sponsoring chocolate company) was introduced for the winner of the <a href="/wiki/Mountains_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Mountains classification in the Tour de France">Mountains Classification</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gard_Obit_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gard_Obit-72">[67]</a></sup> He helped drive an internationalization of the Tour de France, and cycling in general.<sup id="cite_ref-Gard_Obit_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gard_Obit-72">[67]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/1982_Tour_de_France" title="1982 Tour de France">1982</a> <a href="/wiki/Sean_Kelly_(cyclist)" title="Sean Kelly (cyclist)">Sean Kelly</a> of <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a> (points) and <a href="/wiki/Phil_Anderson_(cyclist)" title="Phil Anderson (cyclist)">Phil Anderson</a> of <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> (young rider) became the first winners of any Tour classifications from outside cycling's <a href="/wiki/Continental_Europe" title="Continental Europe">Continental Europe</a> heartlands, while Lévitan was influential in facilitating the participation in the <a href="/wiki/1983_Tour_de_France" title="1983 Tour de France">1983 Tour</a> by amateur riders from the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gard_Obit_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gard_Obit-72">[67]</a></sup> In 1984, for the first time, the Société du Tour de France organized the <a href="/wiki/Tour_de_France_F%C3%A9minin" class="mw-redirect" title="Tour de France Féminin">Tour de France Féminin</a>, a version for women.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[n 6]</a></sup> It was run in the same weeks as the male version, and won by <a href="/wiki/Marianne_Martin" title="Marianne Martin">Marianne Martin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greg_LeMond" title="Greg LeMond">Greg LeMond</a> of the US became the first non-European winner in the <a href="/wiki/1986_Tour_de_France" title="1986 Tour de France">1986 race</a>.</p>
<p>While the global awareness and popularity of the Tour grew during this time, its finances became stretched.<sup id="cite_ref-CN_obit_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CN_obit-75">[69]</a></sup> Goddet and Lévitan continued to clash over the running of the race.<sup id="cite_ref-CN_obit_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CN_obit-75">[69]</a></sup> Lévitan launched the Tour of America, as a precursor to his plans to take the Tour de France to the US.<sup id="cite_ref-CN_obit_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CN_obit-75">[69]</a></sup> The Tour of America lost a lot of money, and it appeared to have been cross-financed by the Tour de France.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoddet1991_56-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoddet1991-56">[51]</a></sup> In the years before 1987, Lévitan's position had always been protected by <a href="/wiki/%C3%89milien_Amaury" title="Émilien Amaury">Émilien Amaury</a>, the then owner of <a href="/wiki/Amaury_Sport_Organisation" title="Amaury Sport Organisation">ASO</a>, but recently, Émilien Amaury had retired and his son <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Amaury" title="Philippe Amaury">Philippe Amaury</a> was now responsible. When Lévitan arrived at his office on 17 March 1987, he found that his doors were locked and he was fired. The organisation of the 1987 Tour de France was taken over by Jean-François Naquet-Radiguet.<sup id="cite_ref-Gann-1987_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gann-1987-76">[70]</a></sup> He was not successful in acquiring more funds, and was fired within one year.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span id="1988–present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1988.E2.80.93present">1988–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 1988–present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(July 2015)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Months before the start of the 1988 Tour, director Jean-François Naquet-Radiguet was replaced by Xavier Louy.<sup id="cite_ref-Gann-1988_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gann-1988-77">[71]</a></sup> In 1988 the Tour was organised by Jean-Pierre Courcol, the director of <i>L'Équipe</i>, then in 1989 by Jean-Pierre Carenso and then by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Marie_Leblanc" title="Jean-Marie Leblanc">Jean-Marie Leblanc</a>, who in 1989 had been race director. The former television presenter <a href="/wiki/Christian_Prudhomme" title="Christian Prudhomme">Christian Prudhomme</a>&#160;— he commentated on the Tour among other events&#160;— replaced Leblanc in 2007, having been assistant director for three years. In 1993 ownership of <i>L'Équipe</i> moved to the <a href="/wiki/Amaury_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Amaury Group">Amaury Group</a>, which formed <a href="/wiki/Amaury_Sport_Organisation" title="Amaury Sport Organisation">Amaury Sport Organisation</a> (ASO) to oversee its sports operations, although the Tour itself is operated by its subsidiary the Société du Tour de France. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199687_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199687-78">[72]</a></sup> ASO employs around 70 people full-time, in an office facing but not connected to <i>L'Équipe</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Issy-les-Moulineaux" title="Issy-les-Moulineaux">Issy-les-Moulineaux</a> area of outer western Paris. That number expands to about 220 during the race itself, not including 500 contractors employed to move barriers, erect stages, signpost the route and other work.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare201337_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare201337-79">[73]</a></sup> ASO now also operate several other major bike races throughout the year.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classifications">Classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Classifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The oldest and main competition in the Tour de France is known as the "general classification", for which the yellow jersey is awarded: the winner of this is said to have won the race.<sup id="cite_ref-SL_exp_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SL_exp-80">[74]</a></sup> A few riders from each team aim to win overall but there are three further competitions to draw riders of all specialties: points, mountains, and a classification for young riders with general classification aspirations.<sup id="cite_ref-SL_exp_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SL_exp-80">[74]</a></sup> The leader of each of the aforementioned classifications wears a distinctive jersey, with riders leading multiple classifications wearing the jersey of the most prestigious that he leads.<sup id="cite_ref-SL_exp_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SL_exp-80">[74]</a></sup> In addition to these four classifications, there are several minor and discontinued classifications that are competed for during the race.<sup id="cite_ref-SL_exp_80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SL_exp-80">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_classification">General classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: General classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/General_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="General classification in the Tour de France">General classification in the Tour de France</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Tour_de_France_general_classification_winners" title="List of Tour de France general classification winners">List of Tour de France general classification winners</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yellow_jersey_statistics" title="Yellow jersey statistics">Yellow jersey statistics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Voigt_Cancellara_TDF_2010_Cambrai_(cropped).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Voigt_Cancellara_TDF_2010_Cambrai_%28cropped%29.JPG/170px-Voigt_Cancellara_TDF_2010_Cambrai_%28cropped%29.JPG" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Voigt_Cancellara_TDF_2010_Cambrai_%28cropped%29.JPG/255px-Voigt_Cancellara_TDF_2010_Cambrai_%28cropped%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Voigt_Cancellara_TDF_2010_Cambrai_%28cropped%29.JPG/340px-Voigt_Cancellara_TDF_2010_Cambrai_%28cropped%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="639" data-file-height="851" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Voigt_Cancellara_TDF_2010_Cambrai_(cropped).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Fabian_Cancellara" title="Fabian Cancellara">Fabian Cancellara</a> pictured at the <a href="/wiki/2010_Tour_de_France" title="2010 Tour de France">2010 Tour de France</a>. He is the rider who has worn the yellow jersey as leader of the <a href="/wiki/General_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="General classification in the Tour de France">general classification</a> for the most days without ever winning the race.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The oldest and most sought after classification in the Tour de France is the general classification.<sup id="cite_ref-SL_exp_80-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SL_exp-80">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-clasexp_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-clasexp-81">[75]</a></sup> All of the stages are timed to the finish.<sup id="cite_ref-clasexp_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-clasexp-81">[75]</a></sup> The riders' times are compounded with their previous stage times; so the rider with the lowest aggregate time is the leader of the race.<sup id="cite_ref-SL_exp_80-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SL_exp-80">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-clasexp_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-clasexp-81">[75]</a></sup> The leader is determined after each stage's conclusion: he gains the privilege to wear the yellow jersey "maillot jaune", presented on a podium in the stage's finishing town, for the next stage. If a rider is leading more than one classification that awards a jersey, he wears the yellow one, since the general classification is the most important one in the race.<sup id="cite_ref-ASO_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ASO-11">[11]</a></sup> Between <a href="/wiki/1905_Tour_de_France" title="1905 Tour de France">1905</a> and <a href="/wiki/1912_Tour_de_France" title="1912 Tour de France">1912</a> inclusive, in response to concerns about rider cheating in the <a href="/wiki/1904_Tour_de_France" title="1904 Tour de France">1904 race</a>, the general classification was awarded according to a point-based system based on their placings in each stage, and the rider with the lowest total of points after the Tour's conclusion was the winner.<sup id="cite_ref-clasexp_81-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-clasexp-81">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>The leader in the <a href="/wiki/1903_Tour_de_France" title="1903 Tour de France">first Tour de France</a> was awarded a yellow armband.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007-16">[15]</a></sup> The color yellow was chosen as the magazine that created the Tour, <i><a href="/wiki/L%27Auto" class="mw-redirect" title="L'Auto">L'Auto</a></i>, printed its newspapers on yellow paper. The yellow jersey was added to the race in the 1919 edition and it has since become a symbol of the Tour de France.<sup id="cite_ref-SL_exp_80-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SL_exp-80">[74]</a></sup> The first rider to wear the yellow jersey was <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Christophe" title="Eugène Christophe">Eugène Christophe</a>. Each team brings multiple yellow jerseys in advance of the Tour in case one of their riders becomes the overall leader of the race. Riders usually try to make the extra effort to keep the jersey for as long as possible in order to get more publicity for the team and its sponsors. <a href="/wiki/Eddy_Merckx" title="Eddy Merckx">Eddy Merckx</a> has worn the yellow jersey for 96 stages, which is more than any other rider in the history of the Tour de France. Four riders have won the general classification five times in their career: <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Anquetil" title="Jacques Anquetil">Jacques Anquetil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eddy_Merckx" title="Eddy Merckx">Eddy Merckx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Hinault" title="Bernard Hinault">Bernard Hinault</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Miguel_Indurain" class="mw-redirect" title="Miguel Indurain">Miguel Indurain</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mountains_classification">Mountains classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Mountains classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mountains_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Mountains classification in the Tour de France">Mountains classification in the Tour de France</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_Virenque_-_Tour_de_France_2003_-_Alpe_d%27Huez_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Richard_Virenque_-_Tour_de_France_2003_-_Alpe_d%27Huez_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Richard_Virenque_-_Tour_de_France_2003_-_Alpe_d%27Huez_%28cropped%29.jpg" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Richard_Virenque_-_Tour_de_France_2003_-_Alpe_d%27Huez_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Richard_Virenque_-_Tour_de_France_2003_-_Alpe_d%27Huez_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/eb/Richard_Virenque_-_Tour_de_France_2003_-_Alpe_d%27Huez_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="273" data-file-height="363" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_Virenque_-_Tour_de_France_2003_-_Alpe_d%27Huez_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Richard_Virenque" title="Richard Virenque">Richard Virenque</a> pictured at the <a href="/wiki/2003_Tour_de_France" title="2003 Tour de France">2003 Tour de France</a> wearing the polka dot jersey. He won the <a href="/wiki/Mountains_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Mountains classification in the Tour de France">mountains classification</a> a record seven times.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The mountains classification is the second oldest jersey awarding classification in the Tour de France. The mountains classification was added to the Tour de France in the <a href="/wiki/1933_Tour_de_France" title="1933 Tour de France">1933 edition</a> and was first won by <a href="/wiki/Vicente_Trueba" title="Vicente Trueba">Vicente Trueba</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SL_exp_80-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SL_exp-80">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007203_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007203-82">[76]</a></sup> Prizes for the classification were first awarded in <a href="/wiki/1934_Tour_de_France" title="1934 Tour de France">1934</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007203_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007203-82">[76]</a></sup> During stages of the race containing climbs, points are awarded to the first riders to reach the top of each categorized climb, with points available for up to the first 10 riders, depending on the classification of the climb. Climbs are classified according to the steepness and length of that particular hill, with more points available for harder climbs. The classification was preceded by the <i>meilleur grimpeur</i> (English: best climber) which was awarded by the organising newspaper <i>l'Auto</i> to a cyclist who completed each race.</p>
<p>The classification awarded no jersey to the leader until the <a href="/wiki/1975_Tour_de_France" title="1975 Tour de France">1975 Tour de France</a>, when the organizers decided to award a distinctive white jersey with red dots to the leader.<sup id="cite_ref-SL_exp_80-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SL_exp-80">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007203_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007203-82">[76]</a></sup> The climbers' jersey is worn by the rider who, at the start of each stage, has the largest amount of climbing points.<sup id="cite_ref-clasexp_81-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-clasexp-81">[75]</a></sup> If a rider leads two or more of classifications, the climbers' jersey is worn by the rider in second, or third, place in that contest. At the end of the Tour, the rider holding the most climbing points wins the classification. Some riders may race with the aim of winning this particular competition, while others who gain points early on may shift their focus to the classification during the race. The Tour has five categories for ranking the mountains the race covers. The scale ranges from category 4, the easiest, to hors catégorie, the hardest. During his career <a href="/wiki/Richard_Virenque" title="Richard Virenque">Richard Virenque</a> won the mountains classification a record seven times.</p>
<p>The point distribution for the mountains is as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[77]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Points_classification">Points classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Points classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Points_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Points classification in the Tour de France">Points classification in the Tour de France</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alessandro_Petacchi_TDF_2010_Paris.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Alessandro_Petacchi_TDF_2010_Paris.JPG/220px-Alessandro_Petacchi_TDF_2010_Paris.JPG" width="220" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Alessandro_Petacchi_TDF_2010_Paris.JPG/330px-Alessandro_Petacchi_TDF_2010_Paris.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Alessandro_Petacchi_TDF_2010_Paris.JPG/440px-Alessandro_Petacchi_TDF_2010_Paris.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1276" data-file-height="1284" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alessandro_Petacchi_TDF_2010_Paris.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Petacchi" title="Alessandro Petacchi">Alessandro Petacchi</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Points_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Points classification in the Tour de France">green jersey</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The points classification is the third oldest of the currently awarded jersey classifications.<sup id="cite_ref-SL_exp_80-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SL_exp-80">[74]</a></sup> It was introduced in the <a href="/wiki/1953_Tour_de_France" title="1953 Tour de France">1953 Tour de France</a> and was first won by <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Sch%C3%A4r" title="Fritz Schär">Fritz Schär</a>. The classification was added to draw the participation of the sprinters as well as celebrate the 50th anniversary of the Tour. Points are given to the first 15 riders to finish a stage, with an additional set of points given to the first 15 riders to cross a pre-determined 'sprint' point during the route of each stage. The point classification leader green jersey is worn by the rider who at the start of each stage, has the greatest number of points.<sup id="cite_ref-clasexp_81-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-clasexp-81">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the first years, the cyclist received penalty points for not finishing with a high place, so the cyclist with the fewest points was awarded the green jersey. From 1959 on, the system was changed so the cyclists were awarded points for high place finishes (with first place getting the most points, and lower placings getting successively fewer points), so the cyclist with the most points was awarded the green jersey. The amount of points awarded varies depending on the type of stage, with flat stages awarding the most points at the finish and time trials and high mountain stages awarding the least amount of points at the finish.<sup id="cite_ref-clasexp_81-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-clasexp-81">[75]</a></sup> This increases the likelihood of a sprinter winning the points classification, though other riders can be competitive for the classification if they have a sufficient number of high-place finishes.</p>
<p>The winner of the classification is the rider with the most points at the end of the Tour. In case of a tie, the leader is determined by the number of stage wins, then the number of intermediate sprint victories, and finally, the rider's standing in the general classification. The classification has been won a record six times by <a href="/wiki/Erik_Zabel" title="Erik Zabel">Erik Zabel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SL_exp_80-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SL_exp-80">[74]</a></sup> The most recent winner, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Sagan" title="Peter Sagan">Peter Sagan</a>, won it in <a href="/wiki/2012_Tour_de_France" title="2012 Tour de France">2012</a>, <a href="/wiki/2013_Tour_de_France" title="2013 Tour de France">2013</a>, <a href="/wiki/2014_Tour_de_France" title="2014 Tour de France">2014</a>, <a href="/wiki/2015_Tour_de_France" title="2015 Tour de France">2015</a>, and <a href="/wiki/2016_Tour_de_France" title="2016 Tour de France">2016</a>.</p>
<p>The first year the points classification was used it was sponsored by La Belle Jardinière, a lawn mower producer, and the jersey was made green. In <a href="/wiki/1968_Tour_de_France" title="1968 Tour de France">1968</a> the jersey was changed to red to please the sponsor.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[78]</a></sup> However, the color was changed back the following year. For almost 25 years the classification was sponsored by Pari Mutuel Urbain, a state betting company.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007203_82-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007203-82">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-skoda_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skoda-85">[79]</a></sup> However they announced in November 2014 that they would not be continuing their sponsorship, and in March 2015 it was revealed that the green jersey would now be sponsored by Czech car manufacturer <a href="/wiki/%C5%A0koda_Auto" title="Škoda Auto">Škoda</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-skoda_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-skoda-85">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of <a href="/wiki/2015_Tour_de_France" title="2015 Tour de France">2015</a>, the points awarded stands as:<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[80]</a></sup></p>
