<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Young_rider_classification">Young rider classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Young rider classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Young_rider_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Young rider classification in the Tour de France">Young rider classification in the Tour de France</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andy_Schleck_Tour_de_France_2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Andy_Schleck_Tour_de_France_2009.jpg/170px-Andy_Schleck_Tour_de_France_2009.jpg" width="170" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Andy_Schleck_Tour_de_France_2009.jpg/255px-Andy_Schleck_Tour_de_France_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Andy_Schleck_Tour_de_France_2009.jpg/340px-Andy_Schleck_Tour_de_France_2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="371" data-file-height="526" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andy_Schleck_Tour_de_France_2009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Andy_Schleck" title="Andy Schleck">Andy Schleck</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Young_rider_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Young rider classification in the Tour de France">white jersey</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The leader of the classification is determined the same way as the general classification, with the riders' times being added up after each stage and the eligible rider with lowest aggregate time is dubbed the leader. The Young rider classification is restricted to the riders that are under the age of 26. Originally the classification was restricted to neo-professionals – riders that are in their first three years of professional racing – until <a href="/wiki/1983_Tour_de_France" title="1983 Tour de France">1983</a>. In 1983, the organizers made it so that only first time riders were eligible for the classification. In <a href="/wiki/1987_Tour_de_France" title="1987 Tour de France">1987</a>, the organizers changed the rules of the classification to what they are today.</p>
<p>This classification was added to the Tour de France in the <a href="/wiki/1975_Tour_de_France" title="1975 Tour de France">1975 edition</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Moser" title="Francesco Moser">Francesco Moser</a> being the first to win the classification after placing seventh overall. The Tour de France awards a white jersey to the leader of the classification, although this was not done between 1989 and 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-SL_exp_80-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SL_exp-80">[74]</a></sup> Four riders have won both the young rider classification and the general classification in the same year: <a href="/wiki/Laurent_Fignon" title="Laurent Fignon">Laurent Fignon</a> (1983), <a href="/wiki/Jan_Ullrich" title="Jan Ullrich">Jan Ullrich</a> (<a href="/wiki/1997_Tour_de_France" title="1997 Tour de France">1997</a>), <a href="/wiki/Alberto_Contador" title="Alberto Contador">Alberto Contador</a> (<a href="/wiki/2007_Tour_de_France" title="2007 Tour de France">2007</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Andy_Schleck" title="Andy Schleck">Andy Schleck</a> (2010). Two riders have won the young rider classification three times in their respective careers: Jan Ullrich and Andy Schleck.</p>
<p>As of 2015 Jersey sponsor is Optician company Krys,<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[81]</a></sup> replacing Škoda who moved to the Green Jersey.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minor_classifications_and_prizes">Minor classifications and prizes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Minor classifications and prizes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Combativity_award_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Combativity award in the Tour de France">prix de la combativité</a></i> goes to the rider who most animates the day, usually by trying to break clear of the field. The most combative rider wears a number printed white-on-red instead of black-on-white next day. An award goes to the most aggressive rider throughout the Tour. Already in 1908 a sort of combativity award was offered, when <i>Sports Populaires</i> and <i>L'Education Physique</i> created <i>Le Prix du Courage</i>, 100 francs and a silver gilt medal for "the rider having finished the course, even if unplaced, who is particularly distinguished for the energy he has used."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland200796_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland200796-88">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThompson200840_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThompson200840-89">[83]</a></sup> The modern competition started in 1958.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland200796_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland200796-88">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199645_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199645-90">[84]</a></sup> In 1959, a Super Combativity award for the most combative cyclist of the Tour was awarded. It was initially not awarded every year, but since 1981 it has been given annually. Eddy Merckx has the most wins (4) for the overall award.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Team_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Team classification in the Tour de France">team classification</a> is assessed by adding the time of each team's best three riders each day. The competition does not have its own jersey but since 2006 the leading team has worn numbers printed black-on-yellow. Until 1990, the leading team would wear yellow caps. As of 2012, the riders of the leading team wear yellow helmets.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[85]</a></sup> During the era of national teams, France and Belgium won 10 times each.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007203_82-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007203-82">[76]</a></sup> From 1973 up to 1988, there was also a team classification based on points (stage classification); members of the leading team would wear green caps.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_classifications">Historical classifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Historical classifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There has been an <a href="/wiki/Intermediate_sprints_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Intermediate sprints classification in the Tour de France">intermediate sprints classification</a>, which from 1984 awarded a red jersey<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199677_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199677-92">[86]</a></sup> for points awarded to the first three to pass intermediate points during the stage. These sprints also scored points towards the points classification and bonuses towards the general classification. The intermediate sprints classification with its red jersey was abolished in 1989,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[87]</a></sup> but the intermediate sprints have remained, offering points for the points classification and, until 2007, time bonuses for the general classification.</p>
<p>From 1968 there was a <a href="/wiki/Combination_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Combination classification in the Tour de France">combination classification</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199661_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199661-94">[88]</a></sup> scored on a points system based on standings in the general, points and mountains classifications. The design was originally white, then a patchwork with areas resembling each individual jersey design. This was also abolished in 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199683_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199683-95">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lanterne_rouge">Lanterne rouge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Lanterne rouge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The rider who has taken most time is called the <i><a href="/wiki/Lanterne_rouge" title="Lanterne rouge">lanterne rouge</a></i> (<i>red lantern, as in the red light at the back of a vehicle so it can be seen in the dark</i>) and in past years sometimes carried a small red light beneath his <a href="/wiki/Saddle" title="Saddle">saddle</a>. Such was sympathy that he could command higher fees in the races that previously followed the Tour. In 1939 and 1948 the organisers excluded the last rider every day, to encourage more competitive racing.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[n 7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prizes">Prizes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Prizes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TdFPrizeMoney.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/73/TdFPrizeMoney.png/300px-TdFPrizeMoney.png" width="300" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/73/TdFPrizeMoney.png/450px-TdFPrizeMoney.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/73/TdFPrizeMoney.png 2x" data-file-width="470" data-file-height="310" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TdFPrizeMoney.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Prize money in Euros in the Tour de France, not corrected for inflation.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Prize money has always been awarded. From 20,000 old <a href="/wiki/French_franc" title="French franc">francs</a> the first year,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007300.E2.80.93304_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007300.E2.80.93304-97">[90]</a></sup> prize money has increased each year, although from 1976 to 1987 the first prize was an apartment offered by a race sponsor. The first prize in 1988 was a car, a studio-apartment, a work of art and 500,000 francs in cash. Prizes only in cash returned in 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199669.E2.80.9383_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199669.E2.80.9383-98">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Prizes and bonuses are awarded for daily placings and final placings at the end of the race. In 2009, the winner received €450,000, while each of the 21 stage winners won €8,000 (€10,000 for the team time-trial stage). The winners of the points classification and mountains classification each win €25,000, the young rider competition and the combativity prize €20,000, and €50,000 for the winner of the <a href="/wiki/Team_classification_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Team classification in the Tour de France">team classification</a> (calculated by adding the cumulative times of the best three riders in each team).<sup id="cite_ref-Letour_Prix_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Letour_Prix-99">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Souvenir_Henri_Desgrange" title="Souvenir Henri Desgrange">Souvenir Henri Desgrange</a>, in memory of the founder of the Tour, is awarded to the first rider over the <a href="/wiki/Col_du_Galibier" title="Col du Galibier">Col du Galibier</a> where his monument stands,<sup id="cite_ref-Letour_Prix_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Letour_Prix-99">[92]</a></sup> or to the first rider over the highest col in the Tour. A similar award, the <a href="/wiki/Souvenir_Jacques_Goddet" title="Souvenir Jacques Goddet">Souvenir Jacques Goddet</a>, is made at the summit of the <a href="/wiki/Col_du_Tourmalet" title="Col du Tourmalet">Col du Tourmalet</a>, at the memorial to <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Goddet" title="Jacques Goddet">Jacques Goddet</a>, Desgrange's successor.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Stages">Stages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Stages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The modern tour typically has 21 stages, one per day.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mass-start_stages">Mass-start stages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Mass-start stages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tour_de_France_2006.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Tour_de_France_2006.JPG/220px-Tour_de_France_2006.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Tour_de_France_2006.JPG/330px-Tour_de_France_2006.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Tour_de_France_2006.JPG/440px-Tour_de_France_2006.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tour_de_France_2006.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A collected peloton in the <a href="/wiki/2006_Tour_de_France" title="2006 Tour de France">2006 Tour de France</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Riders in most stages start together. The first kilometres, the <i>départ fictif</i>, are a rolling start without racing. The real start, the <i>départ réel</i> is announced by the Tour director waving a white flag. Riders are permitted to touch, but not push or nudge, each other. The first to cross the stage finish line wins the stage. Riders are given a time based on the group that they finish in. All riders in a group finish in the same time as the lead rider. This avoids dangerous mass sprints. It is not unusual for the entire field to finish in a group, taking time to cross the line but being credited with the same time. Since 2005, when riders fall or crash within the final 3 kilometres of a stage with a flat finish, they are awarded the same time as the group they were in.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[93]</a></sup> This change encourages riders to sprint to the finish for points awards without fear of losing time to the group. The final kilometre has been indicated since 1906 by a red triangle&#160;– the <i>flamme rouge</i>&#160;– above the road. The first photo-finish was in 1955.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199648_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199648-101">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Tour directors categorise mass-stage starts into 'flat', 'hilly' or 'mountain'.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[95]</a></sup> This affects the points awarded in the sprint classification, whether the 3 kilometer rule is operational, and the permitted disqualification time in which riders must finish (which is the winners' time plus a pre-determined percentage of that time).<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[96]</a></sup> Time bonuses of 10, 6 and 4 seconds are awarded to the first three finishers, though this was not done from 2008 to 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[97]</a></sup> Bonuses were previously also awarded to winners of intermediate sprints.</p>
<p>On flat stages, most riders can stay in the peloton to the finish, and sprint finishes are common. <a href="/wiki/Crosswinds" class="mw-redirect" title="Crosswinds">Crosswinds</a>, the difficulty of long <a href="/wiki/Cobblestone" title="Cobblestone">cobbled</a> sections or crashes are often the major challenges of these stages.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[98]</a></sup> On hilly and particularly mountain stages, there can be major shifts in the general classification, particularly where stages finish at the tops of climbs.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[99]</a></sup> It is not uncommon for riders to lose 30 minutes or to be eliminated after finishing outside the time limit.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Individual_time_trials">Individual time trials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Individual time trials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bradley_Wiggins_Tour_2012_EZF.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Bradley_Wiggins_Tour_2012_EZF.jpg/220px-Bradley_Wiggins_Tour_2012_EZF.jpg" width="220" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Bradley_Wiggins_Tour_2012_EZF.jpg/330px-Bradley_Wiggins_Tour_2012_EZF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Bradley_Wiggins_Tour_2012_EZF.jpg/440px-Bradley_Wiggins_Tour_2012_EZF.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1321" data-file-height="1089" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bradley_Wiggins_Tour_2012_EZF.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bradley_Wiggins" title="Bradley Wiggins">Bradley Wiggins</a> riding the <a href="/wiki/2012_Tour_de_France,_Prologue_to_Stage_10#Stage_9" title="2012 Tour de France, Prologue to Stage 10">stage 9</a> <a href="/wiki/Individual_time_trial" title="Individual time trial">individual time trial</a> of the <a href="/wiki/2012_Tour_de_France" title="2012 Tour de France">2012 Tour de France</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Riders in a time trial compete individually against the clock, each starting at a different time. The first time trial was between La Roche-sur-Yon and Nantes (80&#160;km) in 1934.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199634_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199634-107">[100]</a></sup> The first stage in modern Tours is often a short trial, a <i>prologue</i>, to decide who wears yellow on the opening day. The first prologue was in 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199660_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199660-67">[62]</a></sup> The 1988 event, at La Baule, was called "la préface".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199681_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199681-108">[101]</a></sup> There are usually two or three time trials. The final time trial has sometimes been the final stage, more recently often the penultimate stage. The launch ramp, a sloping start pad for riders, was first used in 1965, at Cologne.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199658_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199658-109">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Team_time_trial">Team time trial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Team time trial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A team <a href="/wiki/Time_trial" title="Time trial">time trial</a> (TTT) is a race against the clock in which each team rides alone. The time is that of the fifth rider of each team: riders more than a bike-length behind their team's fifth rider are awarded their own times. The TTT has been criticised for favouring strong teams and handicapping strong riders in weak teams. The prologue stage in <a href="/wiki/1971_Tour_de_France" title="1971 Tour de France">1971</a> was a team time trial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199660_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199660-67">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Time_limits">Time limits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Time limits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Riders completing a stage may be eliminated if their time exceeds the time limit for the stage. The limit is defined as the winner's time plus a percentage, which depends on the type of stage and the winner's average speed. The time limit can be extended or the elimination can be waived if more than 20% of riders exceed the limit or for other reasons such as collisions or foul weather.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[103]</a></sup> For example, for a stage classed as "Coefficient 1: Stages with no particular difficulty", the time limit is the winner's time plus 3% for an average speed of up to 36&#160;km/h, increasing in 8 steps to 11% for a speed of over 50&#160;km/h, while on a "Coefficient 5: Very difficult short stages" it varies from 11% (up to 30&#160;km/h) to 22% (over 40&#160;km/h).<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_stages">Notable stages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Notable stages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:L%27Alp-d%27Huez.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/L%27Alp-d%27Huez.JPG/220px-L%27Alp-d%27Huez.JPG" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/L%27Alp-d%27Huez.JPG/330px-L%27Alp-d%27Huez.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/L%27Alp-d%27Huez.JPG/440px-L%27Alp-d%27Huez.JPG 2x" data-file-width="609" data-file-height="338" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:L%27Alp-d%27Huez.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Altitude profile of the <a href="/wiki/Alpe_d%27Huez" title="Alpe d'Huez">Alpe d'Huez</a> climb</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The race has finished since 1975 with <a href="/wiki/Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es_stage_in_the_Tour_de_France" title="Champs-Élysées stage in the Tour de France">laps of the Champs-Élysées</a>. This stage rarely challenges the leader because it is flat and the leader usually has too much time in hand to be denied. But in 1987, <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Delgado" title="Pedro Delgado">Pedro Delgado</a> broke away on the Champs to challenge the 40-second lead held by <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Roche" title="Stephen Roche">Stephen Roche</a>. He and Roche finished in the peloton and Roche won the Tour. In modern times, there tends to be a gentlemen's agreement: while the points classification is still contended if possible, the overall classification is not fought over; because of this, it is not uncommon for the de facto winner of the overall classification to ride into Paris holding a glass of champagne.</p>
<p>In 1989 the last stage was a time trial. <a href="/wiki/Greg_LeMond" title="Greg LeMond">Greg LeMond</a> overtook <a href="/wiki/Laurent_Fignon" title="Laurent Fignon">Laurent Fignon</a> to win by eight seconds, the closest margin in the Tour's history.</p>
<p>The climb of <a href="/wiki/Alpe_d%27Huez" title="Alpe d'Huez">Alpe d'Huez</a> has become one of the more noted mountain stages. During the <a href="/wiki/2004_Tour_de_France" title="2004 Tour de France">2004 Tour de France</a> it was the scene of a 15.5&#160;km mountain time trial on the 16th stage. Riders complained of abusive spectators who threatened their progress up the climb.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mont_Ventoux#Tour_de_France" title="Mont Ventoux">Mont Ventoux</a> is often claimed to be the hardest in the Tour because of the harsh conditions. Another notable mountain stage frequently featured climbs the <a href="/wiki/Col_du_Tourmalet#Tour_de_France" title="Col du Tourmalet">Col du Tourmalet</a>, the most visited mountain in the history of the Tour. <a href="/wiki/Col_du_Galibier" title="Col du Galibier">Col du Galibier</a> is the most visited mountain in the Alps. The <a href="/wiki/2011_Tour_de_France" title="2011 Tour de France">2011 Tour de France</a> stage to Galibier marked the 100th anniversary of the mountain in the Tour and also boasted the highest finish altitude ever: 2,645 m.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[107]</a></sup> Some mountain stages have become memorable because of the weather. An example is a stage in <a href="/wiki/1996_Tour_de_France" title="1996 Tour de France">1996 Tour de France</a> from <a href="/wiki/Val-d%27Is%C3%A8re" title="Val-d'Isère">Val-d'Isère</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sestriere" title="Sestriere">Sestriere</a>. A snowstorm at the start area led to a shortening of the stage from 190 to just 46&#160;km.</p>
<p>To host a stage start or finish brings prestige and business to a town. The prologue and first stage (<a href="/wiki/List_of_Tour_de_France_Grands_D%C3%A9parts" title="List of Tour de France Grands Départs">Grand Départ</a>) are particularly prestigious. The race may start with a prologue (too short to go between towns) in which case the start of the next day's racing, which would be considered stage 1, would usually be in the same town. In 2007 director <a href="/wiki/Christian_Prudhomme" title="Christian Prudhomme">Christian Prudhomme</a> said that "in general, for a period of five years we have the Tour start outside France three times and within France twice."<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Advertising_caravan">Advertising caravan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Advertising caravan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tour_de_France_Advertising_Caravan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Tour_de_France_Advertising_Caravan.jpg/220px-Tour_de_France_Advertising_Caravan.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Tour_de_France_Advertising_Caravan.jpg/330px-Tour_de_France_Advertising_Caravan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Tour_de_France_Advertising_Caravan.jpg/440px-Tour_de_France_Advertising_Caravan.jpg 2x" data-file-width="959" data-file-height="646" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tour_de_France_Advertising_Caravan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Vehicles from the 2014 Tour de France Publicity Caravan</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When switching to the use of national teams in 1930, the costs of accommodating riders fell to the organizers instead of the sponsors and Henri Desgrange raised the money by allowing advertisers to precede the race. The procession of often colourfully decorated trucks and cars became known as the publicity caravan. It formalised an existing situation, companies having started to follow the race. The first to sign to precede the Tour was the chocolate company, <a href="/wiki/Menier_Chocolate" title="Menier Chocolate">Menier</a>, one of those who had followed the race. Its head of publicity, Paul Thévenin, had first put the idea to Desgrange.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThierryChany2011242_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThierryChany2011242-116">[109]</a></sup> It paid 50,000 old francs. Preceding the race was more attractive to advertisers because spectators gathered by the road long before the race or could be attracted from their houses. Advertisers following the race found that many who had watched the race had already gone home. Menier handed out tons of chocolate in that first year of preceding the race, as well as 500,000 policemen's hats printed with the company's name. The success led to the caravan's existence being formalised the following year.</p>
<p>The caravan was at its height between 1930 and the mid-1960s, before television and especially television advertising was established in France. Advertisers competed to attract public attention. Motorcycle acrobats performed for the <a href="/wiki/Cinzano" title="Cinzano">Cinzano</a> apéritif company and a toothpaste maker, and an accordionist, Yvette Horner, became one of the most popular sights as she performed on the roof of a Citroën Traction Avant.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[110]</a></sup> The modern Tour restricts the excesses to which advertisers are allowed to go but at first anything was allowed. The writer Pierre Bost<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[n 8]</a></sup> lamented: "This caravan of 60 gaudy trucks singing across the countryside the virtues of an apéritif, a make of underpants or a dustbin is a shameful spectacle. It bellows, it plays ugly music, it's sad, it's ugly, it smells of vulgarity and money."<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Advertisers pay the Société du Tour de France approximately €150,000 to place three vehicles in the caravan.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-120">[112]</a></sup> Some have more. On top of that come the more considerable costs of the commercial samples that are thrown to the crowd and the cost of accommodating the drivers and the staff—frequently students—who throw them. The number of items has been estimated at 11 million, each person in the procession giving out 3,000 to 5,000 items a day.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-120">[112]</a></sup> A bank, GAN, gave out 170,000 caps, 80,000 badges, 60,000 plastic bags and 535,000 copies of its race newspaper in 1994. Together, they weighed 32 tons.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated7_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated7-121">[113]</a></sup> The vehicles also have to be decorated on the morning of each stage and, because they must return to ordinary highway standards, disassembled after each stage. Numbers vary but there are normally around 250 vehicles each year. Their order on the road is established by contract, the leading vehicles belonging to the largest sponsors.</p>
<p>The procession sets off two hours before the start and then regroups to precede the riders by an hour and a half. It spreads 20–25&#160;km and takes 40 minutes to pass at between 20 and 60&#160;km/h. Vehicles travel in groups of five. Their position is logged by <a href="/wiki/GPS" class="mw-redirect" title="GPS">GPS</a> and from an aircraft and organised on the road by the caravan director—Jean-Pierre Lachaud<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[n 9]</a></sup>—an assistant, three motorcyclists, two radio technicians and a breakdown and medical crew.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_120-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-120">[112]</a></sup> Six motorcyclists from the Garde Républicaine, the élite of the gendarmerie, ride with them.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated7_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated7-121">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first three Tours stayed within France. The 1906 race went into <a href="/wiki/Alsace-Lorraine" title="Alsace-Lorraine">Alsace-Lorraine</a>, territory annexed by the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> in 1871 after the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">Franco-Prussian War</a>. Passage was secured through a meeting at <a href="/wiki/Metz" title="Metz">Metz</a> between Desgrange's collaborator, Alphonse Steinès, and the German governor.</p>
<p>No teams from Italy, Germany or Spain rode in 1939 because of tensions preceding the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> (after German assistance to <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Franco" title="Francisco Franco">Franco</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Civil_War" title="Spanish Civil War">Spanish Civil War</a> it was widely expected Spain would join Germany in a European war, though this did not come to pass). Henri Desgrange planned a Tour for 1940, after war had started but before France had been invaded. The route, approved by military authorities, included a route along the <a href="/wiki/Maginot_Line" title="Maginot Line">Maginot Line</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Bicycle.2C_UK.2C_8_July_1943.2C_p6_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Bicycle.2C_UK.2C_8_July_1943.2C_p6-123">[114]</a></sup> Teams would have been drawn from military units in France, including the British, who would have been organised by a journalist, Bill Mills.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Bicycle.2C_UK.2C_8_July_1943.2C_p6_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Bicycle.2C_UK.2C_8_July_1943.2C_p6-123">[114]</a></sup> Then the Germans invaded and the race was not held again until 1947 (see <a href="/wiki/Tour_de_France_during_the_Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Tour de France during the Second World War">Tour de France during the Second World War</a>). The first German team after the war was in 1960, although individual Germans had ridden in mixed teams. The Tour has since started in Germany four times: in Cologne in 1965, in Frankfurt in 1980, in West Berlin on the city's 750th anniversary in 1987 and in Düsseldorf in 2017. Plans to enter <a href="/wiki/German_Democratic_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="German Democratic Republic">East Germany</a> in 1987 were abandoned.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Corsica">Corsica</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Corsica">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Prior to 2013, the Tour de France had visited every region of <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_France" title="Metropolitan France">Metropolitan France</a> except <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-nouvelobs_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nouvelobs-124">[115]</a></sup> Jean-Marie Leblanc, when he was organiser, said the island had never asked for a stage start there. It would be difficult to find accommodation for 4,000 people, he said.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-125">[116]</a></sup> The spokesman of the Corsican nationalist party <a href="/wiki/Party_of_the_Corsican_Nation" title="Party of the Corsican Nation">Party of the Corsican Nation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Alfonsi" title="François Alfonsi">François Alfonsi</a>, said: "The organisers must be afraid of terrorist attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[n 10]</a></sup> If they are really thinking of a possible terrorist action, they are wrong. Our movement, which is nationalist and in favour of self-government, would be delighted if the Tour came to Corsica."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-125">[116]</a></sup> The opening three stages of the <a href="/wiki/2013_Tour_de_France" title="2013 Tour de France">2013 Tour de France</a> were held on Corsica as part of the celebrations for the 100th edition of the race.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_start_and_finish_of_the_Tour">The start and finish of the Tour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: The start and finish of the Tour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Most stages are in mainland France, although since the 1960s it has become common to visit nearby countries:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013149_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013149-127">[117]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andorra" title="Andorra">Andorra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monaco" title="Monaco">Monaco</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> have all hosted stages or part of a stage. Since 1975 the finish has been on the <a href="/wiki/Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es" title="Champs-Élysées">Champs-Élysées</a> in Paris; from 1903 to 1967 the race finished at the <a href="/wiki/Parc_des_Princes" title="Parc des Princes">Parc des Princes</a> stadium in western Paris and from 1968 to 1974 at the <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A9lodrome_de_Vincennes" title="Vélodrome de Vincennes">Piste Municipale</a> south of the capital.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199661_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199661-94">[88]</a></sup> Feliz Levitan, race organizer in the 1980s, was keen to host stages in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, but these proposals have never been developed.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Starts_abroad">Starts abroad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Starts abroad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following editions of the Tour started, or are planned to start, outside France:<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[119]</a></sup></p>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em;">
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/1954_Tour_de_France" title="1954 Tour de France">1954</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/1958_Tour_de_France" title="1958 Tour de France">1958</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/1965_Tour_de_France" title="1965 Tour de France">1965</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/1973_Tour_de_France" title="1973 Tour de France">1973</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Scheveningen" title="Scheveningen">Scheveningen</a>, Netherlands</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/1975_Tour_de_France" title="1975 Tour de France">1975</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Charleroi" title="Charleroi">Charleroi</a>, Belgium</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/1978_Tour_de_France" title="1978 Tour de France">1978</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Leiden" title="Leiden">Leiden</a>, Netherlands</li>
