<li><b><a href="/wiki/1982_Tour_de_France" title="1982 Tour de France">1982</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Basel" title="Basel">Basel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/1987_Tour_de_France" title="1987 Tour de France">1987</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/West_Berlin" title="West Berlin">West Berlin</a>, West Germany</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/1989_Tour_de_France" title="1989 Tour de France">1989</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg_(city)" class="mw-redirect" title="Luxembourg (city)">Luxembourg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/1992_Tour_de_France" title="1992 Tour de France">1992</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/San_Sebasti%C3%A1n" title="San Sebastián">San Sebastián</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/1996_Tour_de_France" title="1996 Tour de France">1996</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/%27s-Hertogenbosch" title="'s-Hertogenbosch">Den Bosch</a>, Netherlands</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/1998_Tour_de_France" title="1998 Tour de France">1998</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Ireland</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/2002_Tour_de_France" title="2002 Tour de France">2002</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg_(city)" class="mw-redirect" title="Luxembourg (city)">Luxembourg</a>, Luxembourg</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/2004_Tour_de_France" title="2004 Tour de France">2004</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Li%C3%A8ge" title="Liège">Liège</a>, Belgium</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/2007_Tour_de_France" title="2007 Tour de France">2007</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/2009_Tour_de_France" title="2009 Tour de France">2009</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Monte_Carlo" title="Monte Carlo">Monte Carlo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monaco" title="Monaco">Monaco</a></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/2010_Tour_de_France" title="2010 Tour de France">2010</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam" title="Rotterdam">Rotterdam</a>, Netherlands</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/2012_Tour_de_France" title="2012 Tour de France">2012</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Li%C3%A8ge" title="Liège">Liège</a>, Belgium</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/2014_Tour_de_France" title="2014 Tour de France">2014</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a>, United Kingdom</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/2015_Tour_de_France" title="2015 Tour de France">2015</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Utrecht" title="Utrecht">Utrecht</a>, Netherlands</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/2017_Tour_de_France" title="2017 Tour de France">2017</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="Düsseldorf">Düsseldorf</a>, Germany</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/2019_Tour_de_France" title="2019 Tour de France">2019</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>, Belgium</li>
</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Broadcasting">Broadcasting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Broadcasting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Tour was first followed only by journalists from <i>L'Auto</i>, the organisers. The race was founded to increase sales of a floundering newspaper and its editor, Desgrange, saw no reason to allow rival publications to profit. The first time papers other than <i>L'Auto</i> were allowed was 1921, when 15 press cars were allowed for regional and foreign reporters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAugendre199621_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAugendre199621-130">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Tour was shown first on cinema newsreels a day or more after the event. The first live radio broadcast was in 1929, when Jean Antoine and Alex Virot of the newspaper <i>L'Intransigeant</i> broadcast for Radio Cité. They used telephone lines. In 1932 they broadcast the sound of riders crossing the col d'Aubisque in the Pyrenees on 12 July, using a recording machine and transmitting the sound later.</p>
<p>The first television pictures were shown a day after a stage. The national TV channel used two 16mm cameras, a Jeep and a motorbike. Film was flown or taken by train to Paris. It was edited there and shown the following day.</p>
<p>The first live broadcast, and the second of any sport in France, was the finish at the Parc des Princes in Paris on 25 July 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013134_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013134-131">[121]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rik_Van_Steenbergen" title="Rik Van Steenbergen">Rik Van Steenbergen</a> of Belgium led in the bunch after a stage of 340&#160;km from <a href="/wiki/Nancy,_France" title="Nancy, France">Nancy</a>. The first live coverage from the side of the road was from the Aubisque on 8 July 1958. Proposals to cover the whole race were abandoned in 1962 after objections from regional newspapers whose editors feared the competition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013136_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013136-132">[122]</a></sup> The dispute was settled but not in time and the first complete coverage was the following year. In 1958 the first mountain climbs were broadcast live on television for the first time,<sup id="cite_ref-Thompson_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson-133">[123]</a></sup> and in 1959 helicopters were first used for the television coverage.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>The leading television commentator in France was a former rider, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Chapatte" title="Robert Chapatte">Robert Chapatte</a>. At first he was the only commentator. He was joined in following seasons by an analyst for the mountain stages and by a commentator following the competitors by motorcycle.</p>
<p>Broadcasting in France was largely a state monopoly until 1982, when the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Party_(France)" title="Socialist Party (France)">socialist</a> president <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Mitterrand" title="François Mitterrand">François Mitterrand</a> allowed private broadcasters and privatised the leading television channel. Competition between channels raised the broadcasting fees paid to the organisers from 1.5 per cent of the race budget in 1960 to more than a third by the end of the century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013117_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDaunceyHare2013117-135">[125]</a></sup> Broadcasting time also increased as channels competed to secure the rights. The two largest channels to stay in public ownership, <a href="/wiki/Antenne_2" class="mw-redirect" title="Antenne 2">Antenne 2</a> and <a href="/wiki/FR3" class="mw-redirect" title="FR3">FR3</a>, combined to offer more coverage than its private rival, <a href="/wiki/TF1" title="TF1">TF1</a>. The two stations, renamed France 2 and France 3, still hold the domestic rights and provide pictures for broadcasters around the world.</p>
<p>The stations use a staff of 300 with four helicopters, two aircraft, two motorcycles, 35 other vehicles including trucks, and 20 podium cameras.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[n 11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Domestic television covers the most important stages of the Tour, such as those in the mountains, from mid-morning until early evening. Coverage typically starts with a survey of the day's route, interviews along the road, discussions of the difficulties and tactics ahead, and a 30-minute archive feature. The biggest stages are shown live from start to end, followed by interviews with riders and others and features such an edited version of the stage seen from beside a team manager following and advising riders from his car. Radio covers the race in updates throughout the day, particularly on the national news channel, <a href="/wiki/France_Info" title="France Info">France Info</a>, and some stations provide continuous commentary on long wave. The 1979 Tour was the first to be broadcast in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>The combination of unprecedented rigorous doping controls and almost no positive tests helped restore fans' confidence in the 2009 Tour de France. This led directly to an increase in global popularity of the event. The most watched stage of 2009 was stage 20, from Montélimar to Mont Ventoux in Provence, with a global total audience of 44 million, making it the 12th most watched sporting event in the world in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[127]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Didi_Senft-Einzelzeitfahren-Deutschlandtour_2005.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Didi_Senft-Einzelzeitfahren-Deutschlandtour_2005.jpg/220px-Didi_Senft-Einzelzeitfahren-Deutschlandtour_2005.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Didi_Senft-Einzelzeitfahren-Deutschlandtour_2005.jpg/330px-Didi_Senft-Einzelzeitfahren-Deutschlandtour_2005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Didi_Senft-Einzelzeitfahren-Deutschlandtour_2005.jpg/440px-Didi_Senft-Einzelzeitfahren-Deutschlandtour_2005.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Didi_Senft-Einzelzeitfahren-Deutschlandtour_2005.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Part of the crowd during most days of the Tour is <a href="/wiki/Didi_Senft" title="Didi Senft">Didi Senft</a> who, in a red <a href="/wiki/Devil" title="Devil">devil</a> costume, has been the Tour devil since 1993.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Tour is important for fans in Europe. Millions<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[128]</a></sup> line the route, some having camped for a week to get the best view. Crowds flanking the course are reminiscent of the community festivals that are part of another form of cycle racing in a different country – the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man_TT" title="Isle of Man TT">Isle of Man TT</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Tour de France appealed from the start not just for the distance and its demands but because it played to a wish for national unity,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoeufL.C3.A9onard200367_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoeufL.C3.A9onard200367-141">[130]</a></sup> a call to what <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Barr%C3%A8s" title="Maurice Barrès">Maurice Barrès</a> called the France "of earth and deaths" or what Georges Vigarello called "the image of a France united by its earth."<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[131]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:G.Bruno01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/G.Bruno01.jpg/220px-G.Bruno01.jpg" width="220" height="315" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/G.Bruno01.jpg/330px-G.Bruno01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/G.Bruno01.jpg/440px-G.Bruno01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="520" data-file-height="744" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:G.Bruno01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
School book by Augustine Fouillée under the 'nom de plume' G. Bruno.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The image had been started by the 1877 travel/school book <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Tour_de_la_France_par_deux_enfants" title="Le Tour de la France par deux enfants">Le Tour de la France par deux enfants</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[n 12]</a></sup> It told of two boys, André and Julien, who "in a thick September fog left the town of <a href="/wiki/Phalsbourg" title="Phalsbourg">Phalsbourg</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lorraine_(region)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lorraine (region)">Lorraine</a> to see France at a time when few people had gone far beyond their nearest town."</p>
<p>The book sold six million copies by the time of the first Tour de France,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoeufL.C3.A9onard200367_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoeufL.C3.A9onard200367-141">[130]</a></sup> the biggest selling book of 19th-century France (other than the Bible).<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[132]</a></sup> It stimulated a national interest in France, making it "visible and alive", as its preface said. There had already been a car race called the Tour de France but it was the publicity behind the cycling race, and Desgrange's drive to educate and improve the population,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoeufL.C3.A9onard200370_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoeufL.C3.A9onard200370-145">[133]</a></sup> that inspired the French to know more of their country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoeufL.C3.A9onard200374_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoeufL.C3.A9onard200374-146">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>The academic historians Jean-Luc Boeuf and Yves Léonard say most people in France had little idea of the shape of their country until <i>L'Auto</i> began publishing maps of the race.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBoeufL.C3.A9onard200375-76_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoeufL.C3.A9onard200375-76-147">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts">Arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Tour has inspired several popular songs in France, notably <i>P'tit gars du Tour</i> (1932), <i>Les Tours de France</i> (1936) and <i>Faire le Tour de France</i> (1950). <a href="/wiki/Kraftwerk" title="Kraftwerk">Kraftwerk</a> had a hit with "<a href="/wiki/Tour_de_France_(song)" title="Tour de France (song)">Tour de France</a>" in 1983&#160;– described as a minimalistic "melding of man and machine"<sup id="cite_ref-kraftwerk_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kraftwerk-148">[136]</a></sup>&#160;– and produced an album, <i><a href="/wiki/Tour_de_France_Soundtracks" title="Tour de France Soundtracks">Tour de France Soundtracks</a></i> in 2003, the centenary of the Tour.</p>
<p>The Tour and its first Italian winner, <a href="/wiki/Ottavio_Bottecchia" title="Ottavio Bottecchia">Ottavio Bottecchia</a>, are mentioned at the end of <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Hemingway" title="Ernest Hemingway">Ernest Hemingway</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sun_Also_Rises" title="The Sun Also Rises">The Sun Also Rises</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>In films, the Tour was background for <i><a href="/wiki/Five_Red_Tulips" title="Five Red Tulips">Five Red Tulips</a></i> (1949) by <a href="/wiki/Jean_Stelli" title="Jean Stelli">Jean Stelli</a>, in which five riders are murdered. A burlesque in 1967, <i>Les Cracks</i> by Alex Joffé, with Bourvil et Monique Tarbès, also featured him. Patrick Le Gall made <i>Chacun son Tour</i> (1996). The comedy, <i>Le Vélo de Ghislain Lambert</i> (2001), featured the Tour of 1974.</p>
<p>In 2005, three films chronicled a team. The German <i>Höllentour</i>, translated as <i><a href="/wiki/Hell_on_Wheels_(2004_film)" title="Hell on Wheels (2004 film)">Hell on Wheels</a></i>, recorded 2003 from the perspective of <a href="/wiki/Team_Columbia" class="mw-redirect" title="Team Columbia">Team Telekom</a>. The film was directed by Pepe Danquart, who won an Academy Award for live-action short film in 1993 for <i>Black Rider</i> (<i>Schwarzfahrer</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[138]</a></sup> The Danish film <i>Overcoming</i> by Tómas Gislason recorded the 2004 Tour from the perspective of <a href="/wiki/Team_CSC" class="mw-redirect" title="Team CSC">Team CSC</a>.</p>
<p><i>Wired to Win</i> chronicles <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7aise_des_Jeux_(cycling_team)" class="mw-redirect" title="Française des Jeux (cycling team)">Française des Jeux</a> riders <a href="/wiki/Baden_Cooke" title="Baden Cooke">Baden Cooke</a> and Jimmy Caspar in 2003. By following their quest for the points classification, won by Cooke, the film looks at the working of the brain. The film, made for IMAX theaters, appeared in December 2005. It was directed by Bayley Silleck, who was nominated for an Academy Award for documentary short subject in 1996 for <i><a href="/wiki/Cosmic_Voyage" title="Cosmic Voyage">Cosmic Voyage</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>A fan, Scott Coady, followed the 2000 Tour with a handheld video camera to make <i>The Tour Baby!</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[140]</a></sup> which raised $160,000 to benefit the <a href="/wiki/Lance_Armstrong_Foundation" class="mw-redirect" title="Lance Armstrong Foundation">Lance Armstrong Foundation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[141]</a></sup> and made a 2005 sequel, <i>Tour Baby Deux!</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Vive Le Tour</i> by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Malle" title="Louis Malle">Louis Malle</a> is an 18-minute short of 1962. The 1965 Tour was filmed by <a href="/wiki/Claude_Lelouch" title="Claude Lelouch">Claude Lelouch</a> in <i>Pour un Maillot Jaune</i>. This 30-minute documentary has no narration and relies on sights and sounds of the Tour.</p>
<p>In fiction, the 2003 animated feature <i>Les Triplettes de Belleville</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Triplets_of_Belleville" title="The Triplets of Belleville">The Triplets of Belleville</a></i>) ties into the Tour de France.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Tour_criteria">Post-Tour criteria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Post-Tour criteria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the Tour de France there are <a href="/wiki/Criterium" title="Criterium">criteria</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>. These races are public spectacles where thousands of people can see their <i>heroes</i>, from the Tour de France, race. The budget of a criterium is over 100,000 Euro, with most of the money going to the riders. Jersey winners or big-name riders earn between 20 and 60 thousand euros per race in start money.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Doping">Doping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Doping">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Doping_at_the_Tour_de_France" title="Doping at the Tour de France">Doping at the Tour de France</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Festina_affair" title="Festina affair">Festina affair</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doping_at_the_1998_Tour_de_France" title="Doping at the 1998 Tour de France">Doping at the 1998 Tour de France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doping_at_the_1999_Tour_de_France" title="Doping at the 1999 Tour de France">Doping at the 1999 Tour de France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Floyd_Landis_doping_case" title="Floyd Landis doping case">Floyd Landis doping case</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Doping_at_the_2007_Tour_de_France" title="Doping at the 2007 Tour de France">Doping at the 2007 Tour de France</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tour_de_Doping.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Tour_de_Doping.jpg/220px-Tour_de_Doping.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Tour_de_Doping.jpg/330px-Tour_de_Doping.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Tour_de_Doping.jpg/440px-Tour_de_Doping.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tour_de_Doping.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Spectators' banner during the <a href="/wiki/2006_Tour_de_France" title="2006 Tour de France">2006 Tour de France</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Allegations of <a href="/wiki/Doping_in_sport" title="Doping in sport">doping</a> have plagued the Tour almost since 1903. Early riders consumed <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a> and used <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">ether</a>, to dull the pain.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[144]</a></sup> Over the years they began to increase performance and the <a href="/wiki/Union_Cycliste_Internationale" title="Union Cycliste Internationale">Union Cycliste Internationale</a> and governments enacted policies to combat the practice.</p>
<p>In 1924, <a href="/wiki/Henri_P%C3%A9lissier" title="Henri Pélissier">Henri Pélissier</a> and his brother <a href="/wiki/Charles_P%C3%A9lissier" title="Charles Pélissier">Charles</a> told the journalist <a href="/wiki/Albert_Londres" title="Albert Londres">Albert Londres</a> they used <a href="/wiki/Strychnine" title="Strychnine">strychnine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chloroform" title="Chloroform">chloroform</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aspirin" title="Aspirin">aspirin</a>, "horse ointment" and other drugs.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[145]</a></sup> The story was published in <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Petit_Parisien" title="Le Petit Parisien">Le Petit Parisien</a></i> under the title <i>Les Forçats de la Route</i> ('The Convicts of the Road')<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007-16">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Mondenard2003_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Mondenard2003-158">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-abcc01_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abcc01-159">[147]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2011145_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2011145-160">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 13 July 1967, British cyclist <a href="/wiki/Tom_Simpson" title="Tom Simpson">Tom Simpson</a> died climbing <a href="/wiki/Mont_Ventoux" title="Mont Ventoux">Mont Ventoux</a> after taking <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">amphetamine</a>.</p>
<p>In 1998, the "Tour of Shame", <a href="/wiki/Willy_Voet" title="Willy Voet">Willy Voet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soigneur" class="mw-redirect" title="Soigneur">soigneur</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Festina" title="Festina">Festina</a> team, was arrested with <a href="/wiki/Erythropoietin" title="Erythropoietin">erythropoietin</a> (EPO), <a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone" title="Growth hormone">growth hormones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Testosterone_(medication)" title="Testosterone (medication)">testosterone</a> and amphetamine. Police raided team hotels and found products in the possession of the cycling team <a href="/wiki/TVM_(cycling_team)" title="TVM (cycling team)">TVM</a>. Riders went on strike. After mediation by director <a href="/wiki/Jean-Marie_Leblanc" title="Jean-Marie Leblanc">Jean-Marie Leblanc</a>, police limited their tactics and riders continued. Some riders had dropped out and only 96 finished the race. It became clear in a trial that management and health officials of the Festina team had organised the doping.</p>
<p>Further measures were introduced by race organisers and the <a href="/wiki/Union_Cycliste_Internationale" title="Union Cycliste Internationale">UCI</a>, including more frequent testing and tests for <a href="/wiki/Blood_doping" title="Blood doping">blood doping</a> (<a href="/wiki/Blood_transfusion" title="Blood transfusion">transfusions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erythropoietin" title="Erythropoietin">EPO</a> use). This would lead the UCI to becoming a particularly interested party in an <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a> initiative, the <a href="/wiki/World_Anti-Doping_Agency" title="World Anti-Doping Agency">World Anti-Doping Agency</a> (WADA), created in 1999. In 2002, the wife of <a href="/wiki/Raimondas_Rum%C5%A1as" title="Raimondas Rumšas">Raimondas Rumšas</a>, third in the <a href="/wiki/2002_Tour_de_France" title="2002 Tour de France">2002 Tour de France</a>, was arrested after <a href="/wiki/Erythropoietin" title="Erythropoietin">EPO</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anabolic_steroids" class="mw-redirect" title="Anabolic steroids">anabolic steroids</a> were found in her car. Rumšas, who had not failed a test, was not penalised. In 2004, <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Gaumont" title="Philippe Gaumont">Philippe Gaumont</a> said doping was endemic to his <a href="/wiki/Cofidis" title="Cofidis">Cofidis</a> team. Fellow Cofidis rider <a href="/wiki/David_Millar" title="David Millar">David Millar</a> confessed to <a href="/wiki/Erythropoietin" title="Erythropoietin">EPO</a> after his home was raided. In the same year, <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Manzano" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Manzano">Jesus Manzano</a>, a rider with the Kelme team, alleged he had been forced by his team to use banned substances.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>Doping controversy has surrounded <a href="/wiki/Lance_Armstrong" title="Lance Armstrong">Lance Armstrong</a>. In August 2005, one month after Armstrong's seventh consecutive victory, <i>L'Équipe</i> published documents it said showed Armstrong had used EPO in the 1999 race.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[150]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[151]</a></sup> At the same Tour, Armstrong's urine showed traces of a glucocorticosteroid hormone, although below the positive threshold. He said he had used skin cream containing <a href="/wiki/Triamcinolone" title="Triamcinolone">triamcinolone</a> to treat <a href="/wiki/Saddle_sores" class="mw-redirect" title="Saddle sores">saddle sores</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[152]</a></sup> Armstrong said he had received permission from the UCI to use this cream.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrongJenkins2001_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrongJenkins2001-165">[153]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/History_of_Lance_Armstrong_doping_allegations" title="History of Lance Armstrong doping allegations">Further allegations</a> ultimately culminated in the United States Anti Doping Agency (<a href="/wiki/USADA" class="mw-redirect" title="USADA">USADA</a>) disqualifying him from all his victories since 1 August 1998, including his seven consecutive Tour de France victories, and a lifetime ban from competing in professional sports.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[154]</a></sup> He chose not to appeal the decision and in January 2013 he admitted doping in a television interview conducted by <a href="/wiki/Oprah_Winfrey" title="Oprah Winfrey">Oprah Winfrey</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[155]</a></sup> despite having made repeated denials throughout his career.<sup id="cite_ref-ArmstrongNYTimes_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ArmstrongNYTimes-168">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Armstrongcbsnews1_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Armstrongcbsnews1-169">[157]</a></sup> On 1 August 2013, <a href="/wiki/Jan_Ullrich" title="Jan Ullrich">Jan Ullrich</a>—arguably Armstrong's biggest Tour de France rival—reportedly said that Armstrong should have his seven stripped wins reinstated, due to the prevalence of doping at the time. Ullrich had won the <a href="/wiki/1997_Tour_de_France" title="1997 Tour de France">1997 Tour</a> and finished second to Armstrong three times—in <a href="/wiki/2000_Tour_de_France" title="2000 Tour de France">2000</a>, <a href="/wiki/2001_Tour_de_France" title="2001 Tour de France">2001</a> and <a href="/wiki/2003_Tour_de_France" title="2003 Tour de France">2003</a>—but declined to stake a claim for his rival's stripped titles.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2006_Tour_de_France" title="2006 Tour de France">2006 Tour</a> had been plagued by the <a href="/wiki/Operaci%C3%B3n_Puerto_doping_case" title="Operación Puerto doping case">Operación Puerto doping case</a> before it began. Favourites such as <a href="/wiki/Jan_Ullrich" title="Jan Ullrich">Jan Ullrich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Basso" title="Ivan Basso">Ivan Basso</a> were banned by their teams a day before the start. Seventeen riders were implicated. American rider <a href="/wiki/Floyd_Landis" title="Floyd Landis">Floyd Landis</a>, who finished the Tour as holder of the overall lead, had tested positive for <a href="/wiki/Testosterone_(medication)" title="Testosterone (medication)">testosterone</a> after he won stage 17, but this was not confirmed until some two weeks after the race finished. On 30 June 2008 Landis lost his appeal to the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Arbitration_for_Sport" title="Court of Arbitration for Sport">Court of Arbitration for Sport</a>, and <a href="/wiki/%C3%93scar_Pereiro" title="Óscar Pereiro">Óscar Pereiro</a> was named as winner.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 24 May 2007, <a href="/wiki/Erik_Zabel" title="Erik Zabel">Erik Zabel</a> admitted using EPO during the first week of the 1996 Tour,<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[160]</a></sup> when he won the points classification. Following his plea that other cyclists admit to drugs, former winner <a href="/wiki/Bjarne_Riis" title="Bjarne Riis">Bjarne Riis</a> admitted in <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a> on 25 May 2007 that he used EPO regularly from 1993 to 1998, including when he won the 1996 Tour.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[161]</a></sup> His admission meant the top three in 1996 were all linked to doping, two admitting cheating. On 24 July 2007 <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Vinokourov" title="Alexander Vinokourov">Alexander Vinokourov</a> tested positive for a blood transfusion (<a href="/wiki/Blood_doping" title="Blood doping">blood doping</a>) after winning a time trial, prompting his Astana team to pull out and police to raid the team's hotel.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[162]</a></sup> The next day <a href="/wiki/Cristian_Moreni" title="Cristian Moreni">Cristian Moreni</a> tested positive for <a href="/wiki/Testosterone_(medication)" title="Testosterone (medication)">testosterone</a>. His Cofidis team pulled out.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>The same day, leader <a href="/wiki/Michael_Rasmussen" title="Michael Rasmussen">Michael Rasmussen</a> was removed for "violating internal team rules" by missing random tests on 9 May and 28 June. Rasmussen claimed to have been in Mexico. The Italian journalist <a href="/wiki/Davide_Cassani" title="Davide Cassani">Davide Cassani</a> told Danish television he had seen Rasmussen in Italy. The alleged lying prompted Rasmussen's firing by Rabobank.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 11 July 2008 <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Beltr%C3%A1n" title="Manuel Beltrán">Manuel Beltrán</a> tested positive for EPO after the first stage.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[165]</a></sup> On 17 July 2008, <a href="/wiki/Riccardo_Ricc%C3%B2" title="Riccardo Riccò">Riccardo Riccò</a> tested positive for <a href="/wiki/Methoxy_polyethylene_glycol-epoetin_beta" title="Methoxy polyethylene glycol-epoetin beta">continuous erythropoiesis receptor activator</a>, a variant of EPO,<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[166]</a></sup> after the fourth stage. In October 2008, it was revealed that Riccò's teammate and <a href="/wiki/2008_Tour_de_France,_Stage_1_to_Stage_11#Stage_10" title="2008 Tour de France, Stage 1 to Stage 11">Stage 10</a> winner <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Piepoli" title="Leonardo Piepoli">Leonardo Piepoli</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Schumacher" title="Stefan Schumacher">Stefan Schumacher</a><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[167]</a></sup>&#160;– who won both time trials&#160;– and <a href="/wiki/Bernhard_Kohl" title="Bernhard Kohl">Bernhard Kohl</a><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[168]</a></sup>&#160;– third on general classification and King of the Mountains&#160;– had tested positive.</p>
<p>After winning the <a href="/wiki/2010_Tour_de_France" title="2010 Tour de France">2010 Tour de France</a>, it was announced that <a href="/wiki/Alberto_Contador" title="Alberto Contador">Alberto Contador</a> had tested positive for low levels of <a href="/wiki/Clenbuterol" title="Clenbuterol">clenbuterol</a> on 21 July rest day.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[169]</a></sup> On 26 January 2011, the <a href="/wiki/RFEC" class="mw-redirect" title="RFEC">Spanish Cycling Federation</a> proposed a 1-year ban<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[170]</a></sup> but reversed its ruling on 15 February and cleared Contador to race.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[171]</a></sup> Despite a pending appeal by the <a href="/wiki/Union_Cycliste_Internationale" title="Union Cycliste Internationale">UCI</a>, Contador finished 5th overall in the <a href="/wiki/2011_Tour_de_France" title="2011 Tour de France">2011 Tour de France</a>, but in February 2012, Contador was suspended and stripped of his 2010 victory.<sup id="cite_ref-cascontador_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cascontador-184">[172]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the 2012 Tour, the 3rd placed rider from 2011, <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A4nk_Schleck" title="Fränk Schleck">Fränk Schleck</a> tested positive for the banned diuretic <a href="/wiki/Xipamide" title="Xipamide">Xipamide</a> and was immediately disqualified from the Tour.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 2012, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Anti-Doping_Agency" title="United States Anti-Doping Agency">United States Anti-Doping Agency</a> released a report on doping by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Postal_Service_cycling_team" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Postal Service cycling team">U.S. Postal Service cycling team</a>, implicating, amongst others, Armstrong. The report contained <a href="/wiki/Affidavit" title="Affidavit">affidavits</a> from riders including <a href="/wiki/Frankie_Andreu" title="Frankie Andreu">Frankie Andreu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tyler_Hamilton" title="Tyler Hamilton">Tyler Hamilton</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Hincapie" title="George Hincapie">George Hincapie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Floyd_Landis" title="Floyd Landis">Floyd Landis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Levi_Leipheimer" title="Levi Leipheimer">Levi Leipheimer</a>, and others describing widespread use of <a href="/wiki/Erythropoietin" title="Erythropoietin">Erythropoietin</a> (EPO), blood transfusion, testosterone, and other banned practices in several Tours.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[174]</a></sup> In October 2012 the UCI acted upon this report, formally stripping Armstrong of all titles since 1 August 1998, including all seven Tour victories,<sup id="cite_ref-BBCArmstrong_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCArmstrong-187">[175]</a></sup> and announced that his Tour wins would not be reallocated to other riders.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[176]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Deaths">Deaths</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Deaths">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_professional_cyclists_who_died_during_a_race" class="mw-redirect" title="List of professional cyclists who died during a race">List of professional cyclists who died during a race</a></div>
<p>Cyclists who have died during the Tour de France:</p>
<ul>
<li>1910: French racer <a href="/wiki/Adolphe_H%C3%A9li%C3%A8re" title="Adolphe Hélière">Adolphe Hélière</a> drowned at the <a href="/wiki/French_Riviera" title="French Riviera">French Riviera</a> during a rest day.</li>
<li>1935: Spanish racer <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Cepeda_(cyclist)" title="Francisco Cepeda (cyclist)">Francisco Cepeda</a> plunged down a ravine on the <a href="/wiki/Col_du_Galibier" title="Col du Galibier">Col du Galibier</a>.</li>
<li>1967: 13 July, Stage 13: <a href="/wiki/Tom_Simpson" title="Tom Simpson">Tom Simpson</a> died of heart failure during the ascent of Mont Ventoux. <a href="/wiki/Amphetamine" title="Amphetamine">Amphetamines</a> were found in Simpson's jersey and blood.</li>
<li>1995: 18 July, Stage 15: <a href="/wiki/Fabio_Casartelli" title="Fabio Casartelli">Fabio Casartelli</a> crashed at 88&#160;km/h (55&#160;mph) while descending the <a href="/wiki/Col_de_Portet_d%27Aspet" title="Col de Portet d'Aspet">Col de Portet d'Aspet</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>Another seven fatal accidents have occurred:</p>
<ul>
<li>1934: A motorcyclist giving a demonstration in the velodrome of La Roche Sur Yon, to entertain the crowd before the cyclists arrived, died after he crashed at high speed.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[177]</a></sup></li>
<li>1957: 14 July: Motorcycle rider Rene Wagter and passenger Alex Virot, a journalist for <a href="/wiki/Radio_Luxembourg_(French)" class="mw-redirect" title="Radio Luxembourg (French)">Radio Luxembourg</a>, went off a mountain road near <a href="/wiki/Ax-les-Thermes" title="Ax-les-Thermes">Ax-les-Thermes</a>.</li>
<li>1958: An official, Constant Wouters, died from injuries received after sprinter <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Darrigade" title="André Darrigade">André Darrigade</a> collided with him at the <a href="/wiki/Parc_des_Princes" title="Parc des Princes">Parc des Princes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007105_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland2007105-190">[178]</a></sup></li>
<li>1964: Nine people died when a supply van hit a bridge in the <a href="/wiki/Dordogne" title="Dordogne">Dordogne</a> region, resulting in the highest tour-related death toll.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[179]</a></sup></li>
<li>2000: A 12-year-old from <a href="/wiki/Ginasservis" title="Ginasservis">Ginasservis</a>, known as Phillippe, was hit by a car in the Tour de France publicity caravan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland200780_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland200780-192">[180]</a></sup></li>
<li>2002: A seven-year-old boy, Melvin Pompele, died near <a href="/wiki/Retjons" title="Retjons">Retjons</a> after running in front of the caravan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWoodland200780_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWoodland200780-192">[180]</a></sup></li>
<li>2009: 18 July, Stage 14: A spectator in her 60s was struck and killed by a police motorcycle while crossing a road along the route near <a href="/wiki/Wittelsheim" title="Wittelsheim">Wittelsheim</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_120-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-120">[112]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Records_and_statistics">Records and statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Records and statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Tour_de_France_records_and_statistics" title="Tour de France records and statistics">Tour de France records and statistics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yellow_jersey_statistics" title="Yellow jersey statistics">Yellow jersey statistics</a></div>
<p>One rider has been <a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Mountains" title="King of the Mountains">King of the Mountains</a>, won the combination classification, combativity award, the points competition, and the Tour in the same year—<a href="/wiki/Eddy_Merckx" title="Eddy Merckx">Eddy Merckx</a> in 1969, which was also the first year he participated.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>Twice the Tour was won by a racer who never wore the yellow jersey until the race was over. In 1947, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Robic" title="Jean Robic">Jean Robic</a> overturned a three-minute deficit on a 257&#160;km final stage into Paris. In 1968, <a href="/wiki/Jan_Janssen" title="Jan Janssen">Jan Janssen</a> of the Netherlands secured his win in the individual time trial on the last day.</p>
<p>The Tour has been won three times by racers who led the general classification on the first stage and holding the lead all the way to Paris. <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Garin" title="Maurice Garin">Maurice Garin</a> did it during the Tour's very first edition, 1903; he repeated the feat the next year, but the results were nullified by the officials as a response to widespread cheating. <a href="/wiki/Ottavio_Bottecchia" title="Ottavio Bottecchia">Ottavio Bottecchia</a> completed a GC start-to-finish sweep in 1924. And in 1928, <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Frantz" title="Nicolas Frantz">Nicolas Frantz</a> held the GC for the entire race, and at the end, the podium consisted solely of members of his racing team. While no one has equalled this feat since '28, four times a racer has taken over the GC lead on the second stage and carried that lead all the way to Paris. It is worth noting that <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Anquetil" title="Jacques Anquetil">Jacques Anquetil</a> predicted he would hold the maillot jaune from start to finish in 1961, which he did. That year, the first day had two stages, 1.01 Rouen to Versailles and 1.02 Versailles to Versailles. No maillot jaune was awarded after stage 1.01 and at the end of the day Anquetil was in yellow <sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[182]</a></sup>.</p>
<p>The most appearances have been by <a href="/wiki/George_Hincapie" title="George Hincapie">George Hincapie</a> with 17. In light of Hincapie's suspension for use of performance-enhancing drugs, before which he held the mark for most consecutive finishes with sixteen, having completed all but his very first, <a href="/wiki/Joop_Zoetemelk" title="Joop Zoetemelk">Joop Zoetemelk</a> holds the record for the most finishes, having completed all 16 of the Tours that he started.</p>
<p>In the early years of the Tour, cyclists rode individually, and were sometimes forbidden to ride together. This led to large gaps between the winner and the number two. Since the cyclists now tend to stay together in a <a href="/wiki/Peloton" title="Peloton">peloton</a>, the margins of the winner have become smaller, as the difference usually originates from time trials, breakaways or on mountain top finishes, or from being left behind the peloton. The smallest margins between the winner and the second placed cyclists at the end of the Tour is 8s between winner <a href="/wiki/Greg_LeMond" title="Greg LeMond">Greg LeMond</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laurent_Fignon" title="Laurent Fignon">Laurent Fignon</a> in <a href="/wiki/1989_Tour_de_France" title="1989 Tour de France">1989</a>. The largest margin, by comparison, remains that of the first Tour in 1903: 2h 49m 45s between Maurice Garin and <a href="/wiki/Lucien_Pothier" title="Lucien Pothier">Lucien Pothier</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>Three riders have won 8 stages in a single year: <a href="/wiki/Charles_P%C3%A9lissier" title="Charles Pélissier">Charles Pélissier</a> (<a href="/wiki/1930_Tour_de_France" title="1930 Tour de France">1930</a><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[184]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Eddy_Merckx" title="Eddy Merckx">Eddy Merckx</a> (<a href="/wiki/1970_Tour_de_France" title="1970 Tour de France">1970</a>, <a href="/wiki/1974_Tour_de_France" title="1974 Tour de France">1974</a><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[185]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Freddy_Maertens" title="Freddy Maertens">Freddy Maertens</a> (<a href="/wiki/1976_Tour_de_France" title="1976 Tour de France">1976</a><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[186]</a></sup>). <a href="/wiki/Mark_Cavendish" title="Mark Cavendish">Mark Cavendish</a> has the most mass finish stage wins with 30 as of stage 14 in 2016, ahead of <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Darrigade" title="André Darrigade">André Darrigade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Leducq" title="André Leducq">André Leducq</a> with 22, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Faber" title="François Faber">François Faber</a> with 19 and Eddy Merckx with 18.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[187]</a></sup> The youngest Tour de France stage winner is <a href="/wiki/Fabio_Battesini" title="Fabio Battesini">Fabio Battesini</a>, who was 19 when he won one stage in the <a href="/wiki/1931_Tour_de_France" title="1931 Tour de France">1931 Tour de France</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[188]</a></sup></p>
<p>The fastest massed-start stage was in 1999 from Laval to Blois (194.5&#160;km), won by <a href="/wiki/Mario_Cipollini" title="Mario Cipollini">Mario Cipollini</a> at 50.4&#160;km/h.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[189]</a></sup> The fastest time-trial is <a href="/wiki/Rohan_Dennis" title="Rohan Dennis">Rohan Dennis</a>' <a href="/wiki/2015_Tour_de_France,_Stage_1_to_Stage_11#Stage_1" title="2015 Tour de France, Stage 1 to Stage 11">stage 1 of the 2015 Tour de France</a> in <a href="/wiki/Utrecht" title="Utrecht">Utrecht</a>, won at an average of 55.446&#160;km/h (34.5&#160;mph).<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[190]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CN1_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CN1-203">[191]</a></sup> The fastest stage win was by the 2013 Orica GreenEDGE team in a team time-trial. It completed the 25&#160;km in Nice (stage 5) at 57.8&#160;km/h.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[192]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>The longest successful post-war breakaway by a single rider was by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Bourlon" title="Albert Bourlon">Albert Bourlon</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1947_Tour_de_France" title="1947 Tour de France">1947 Tour de France</a>. In the stage Carcassone-Luchon, he stayed away for 253&#160;km.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated9_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated9-206">[194]</a></sup> It was one of seven breakaways longer than 200&#160;km, the last being <a href="/wiki/Thierry_Marie" title="Thierry Marie">Thierry Marie</a>'s 234&#160;km escape in 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated9_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated9-206">[194]</a></sup> Bourlon finished 16&#160;m 30s ahead. This is one of the biggest time gaps but not the greatest. That record belongs to <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9-Luis_Viejo" class="mw-redirect" title="José-Luis Viejo">José-Luis Viejo</a>, who beat the peloton by 22&#160;m 50s in the 1976 stage Montgenèvre-Manosque.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated9_206-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated9-206">[194]</a></sup> He was the fourth and most recent rider to win a stage by more than 20 minutes.</p>
<p>The only rider to win the Tour de France and an <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic</a> gold medal in the same year was <a href="/wiki/Bradley_Wiggins" title="Bradley Wiggins">Bradley Wiggins</a> in 2012. Wiggins also has the record of being the only Tour de France champion to win an Olympic gold medal in the <a href="/wiki/Velodrome" title="Velodrome">velodrome</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Record_winners">Record winners</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Record winners">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Four riders have won five times: <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Anquetil" title="Jacques Anquetil">Jacques Anquetil</a> (FRA), <a href="/wiki/Eddy_Merckx" title="Eddy Merckx">Eddy Merckx</a> (BEL), <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Hinault" title="Bernard Hinault">Bernard Hinault</a> (FRA) and <a href="/wiki/Miguel_Indurain" class="mw-redirect" title="Miguel Indurain">Miguel Indurain</a> (ESP). Indurain achieved the mark with a record five consecutive wins.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Tour_de_France&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">De Dion, Clément and Michelin were particularly concerned with <i>Le Vélo</i>—which reported more than cycling—because its financial backer was one of their commercial rivals, the Darracq company. De Dion believed <i>Le Vélo</i> gave Darracq too much attention and him too little. De Dion was a gentlemanly but outspoken man who already wrote columns for <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Figaro" title="Le Figaro">Le Figaro</a></i>, <i>Le Matin</i> and others. He was also rich and could afford to indulge his whims, which included founding <i>Le Nain Jaune</i> (the yellow gnome), a publication that "...answers no particular need."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Desgrange had first attempted to copy and outdo races run by his rival. In 1901 he revived the Paris-Brest event after a decade's absence. Giffard was the first to suggest a race that lasted several days, new to cycling but established practice in car racing. Unlike other cycle races, it would also be run largely without pacers.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">L'Auto preferred to concentrate on the Coupe Gordon-Bennett car race, even though it wasn't to start for another 48 hours. The choice reflects not only that the Tour de France was an unknown quantity&#160;– only after the first race had finished did it establish a reputation&#160;– but it hints at Desgrange's uncertainty. His position as editor depended on raising sales. That would happen if the Tour succeeded. But the paper and his employers would lose a lot of money if it didn't. Desgrange preferred to keep a distance. He didn't drop the flag at the start and he didn't follow the riders. Reporting was left to Lefèvre, whose idea it had been, who followed the race by bike and by train. Desgrange showed a personal interest in his race only when it looked a success.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The use of false and often colourful names was not unusual. It reflected not only the daring of the enterprise but the slight scandal still associated with riding bicycle races, enough that some preferred to use a false name. The first city-to-city race, from Paris to Rouen, included many made-up names or simply initials. The first woman to finish had entered as "Miss America", despite not being American.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The formula in 1905 was a combination of both time and points. Riders had points deducted for each five minutes lost.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A race for female cyclists similar to the male Tour de France had been organized in 1955, but it was not official.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-96"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-96">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jacques Goddet said in his autobiography that teams were using the rule to eliminate rivals. A rider in last position knew he would be disqualified at the end of the stage. If he dropped out before or during the stage, another competitor became the last and he would leave the race as well. That weakened a rival team, which now had fewer helpers.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-118"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-118">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Pierre Bost was a journalist and playwright known for the prolific film and stage scripts he wrote in the 1940s. He died in 1975.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-122"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-122">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jean-Pierre Lachaud joined the Tour de France caravan in 1983 to distribute publicity for Crédit Lyonnais, the bank that sponsors the yellow jersey. The experience led to his starting his own company, Newsport, which now administers the caravan for the Société du Tour de France</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-126">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some <a href="/wiki/Corsican_nationalism" title="Corsican nationalism">Corsican nationalists</a> have used terrorist methods.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-136"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-136">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A podium camera is not one focused on the winner's podium but a full-scale camera on a mount, or podium.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-143"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-143">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A school book written by Augustine Fouillée under the name G. Bruno and published in 1877, it sold six million by 1900, seven million by 1914 and 8,400,000 by 1976. It was used in schools until the 1950s and is still available.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-119"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-119">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Cette caravane de soixante camions barriolés qui chantent à travers la campagne les vertus d'un apéritif, d'un caleçon ou d'une boîte à ordures fait un honteux spectacle. Cela crie, cela fait de la sale musique, c'est laid, c'est triste, c'est bête, cela sue la vulgarité et l'argent."&#160;– Laget, Serge (1990), La Saga du Tour de France, Découvertes Gaillard, France, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-2-07-053101-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-2-07-053101-1">978-2-07-053101-1</a>. Legend says people in remote areas ran into their houses at the sight of a giant model black lion on the roof of a car promoting Lion Noir shoe polish in 1930.</span></li>
